O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the last of the science of medicine,  its completion having reached its end, and through its expositions and declarations, it seems to me that I should fulfill my obligation to you with this treatise, which is a part of hand surgery, that is, surgery.
Because hand surgery is almost entirely lost in our region and in our time, so that its science is greatly diminished and its practice ceased; and nothing remains of it but some brief descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly altered and have led to errors and hesitation, so that their intentions are obscure and their power and art weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise, according to the method of exposition, explanation, and abbreviation; and so that it may come with the forms of cautery instruments and other surgical instruments, as it should be, by means of added explanation and preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled artisan with his hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be practiced in the science of anatomy, which knowledge is necessary so that he knows the positions of the members and their forms, and their connections and separations; and has knowledge of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, and their number and arrangement; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their origin.
And for this reason, Hippocrates says that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have said concerning anatomy is liable to fall into error by which men will die, as I have seen many of them, because some are trained in this science and practice it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that much blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who was advanced in years, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone along with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and a black slave came to him who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with pads and splints with tight binding, and did not allow the wound to breathe (to heal).
And then he unbound it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it like that for some days and commanded that we not unbind the binding; and it remained thus until the thigh and foot were suppurated, and it came to ruin (the patient died or suffered permanent damage).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the binding, and tranquility was achieved and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, the corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for some days, until the malignancy of it was magnified by its lord (the infection worsened significantly).
The reason is that the burning cancer is pure melancholic humor, therefore it should not be approached with iron at all, but rather it should be in a limb which can be completely amputated (or otherwise entirely removed).
And therefore, son, it is necessary for you that the operation by hand be divided into two parts: that is, into an operation which is associated with health, and into a work in which there is peril in the most favorable conditions (or, "...in the best of circumstances").
And I have already explained this in every part of this book where an operation occurs that involves error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to observe this and leave that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and avoid terrible diseases of difficult healing, and abstain from those things that make you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not overcome you: for it is more lasting for your glory, and higher in the world and in the hereafter for your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called a bad physician".
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head, down to the feet, the forms of instruments and the cautery irons, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incisions and perforations, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and of dislocations, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and of the harms from it, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and the harm from it is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and already a great multitude of wise men have spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and a little of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, which are with matter or without matter, except for two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to that, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not convenient in a disease which is by quality and by dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry disease should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I refute those who say the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry disease that comes to the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, concerning this discourse, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not fitting that this should apply to someone who is already experienced and practiced in the chapter on cautery from prior exercise, and who has recognized the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of the diseases themselves, and their causes, and their accidents, and the duration of those diseases.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid diseases, because all doctors are agreed on those, and are not in disagreement about the benefit of cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness, is superior to cautery with a burning medicine, because fire is simple, whose action does not reach except to the member which is cauterized, and does not harm another member near it except with slight harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches even those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings about in the member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except insofar as it is superfluous. And it is known to us through experiment and long service of study in the art concerning the truth of the causes; and for that reason I am excused from the length of discourse. And if it were not that it is not appropriate for my book to pause here, I would reveal to you the secret of fire, and the quality of its operation in the body and of its expulsion whereby it expels diseases, with philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be burdensome for your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, placing springtime as the best time.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental timing is overwhelmed by the benefit that is obtained with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for necessary, strong, and sudden pains, which do not permit delay because it is feared that greater harm will come from waiting than the slight harm incurred due to timing.
And this, my sons, is not understood except by the vulgar populace and foolish physicians, namely that there is no appropriate time for a disease that is healed by cautery, and they base this on that consequence.
And the cause is not as they believe it to be, for cautery is nothing but a kind of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
However, cautery is superfluous over other medicine because of the speed of its effectiveness and the power of its action and the greatness of its Lord (i.e., its efficacy is so great that it renders other medicine unnecessary).
And it is possible that the illness may return at some time, depending on the patient's constitution and the location of their illness, and their strength, and what is prepared in their body from the accumulation of superfluities within them, and what has reached them through their intake of food and similar causes; unless the illness to which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a limb with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar cases, it is possible that the pain will not return, but this happens rarely.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this is a correct saying, not in the sense that they understand it, for they believe that it is a cure that does not benefit from medicine or any other means besides cautery itself.
And the reason is the opposite; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those medicines do not work, and then, at the end of the process, cautery is administered, and it is beneficial in that case. And therefore, from this we see that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and the many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the evenness of gold and the excellence of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although this is not absolutely true, for I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies and not others, and cautery with this is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you perform cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity you want, because of the hardness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply too much fire to it, the gold melts from the fire, and some of the lamina falls into the fire, so that the craftsman is distracted by this.
And for this reason, cautery with iron is done more easily and more quickly to a correct operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I might easily find what is needed.
The first chapter.
This cauterization counteracts the humidity and coldness on the brain, the accidents of which are caused by two things: that is, the cause of profuse and multiple rheumatic flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleep, and toothache and sore throat, and universally any illness that comes from cold, such as paralysis, swooning, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, depending on the strength of the patient, their age, and their constitution.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a square bench and have him place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the bridge of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that place, mark that spot with ink, and then ignite the olive cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with ink, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand is gradually tightened, and you turn it.
And then lift your hand lightly and examine the place, and if you see some quantity of the bone uncovered, like the amount of a grain of barley, then lift your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron [cautery], with another of those, if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the place and leave it for three days.
And then place on it cotton soaked in vinegar and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the place with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound remains open, it heals better, for that is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone marrow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's thickness is hardened.
And then scrape away what is burned from the bone, and then let it heal.
And others say that with the cautery, penetrate to the back, until it first reaches the strong part of the bone, until the bone cracks, and this is the form of a small ulcer: And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And let the wound be kept open for a long time, and then let it be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a matter of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health, when it is; for the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and use that, and with that work towards health.
Chapter two.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs throughout the head in a patient, and he uses cupping, leeches, purges, plasters, ointments, poultices, and similar remedies, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we describe and none of those things benefit him, then consider whether the patient's head is of a strong constitution by nature; and if it is not cold and finds great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below the previous one. And then cauterize that above the temple of his head with a cautery until the scab is formed. Incise the scab and uncover the bone to the extent that we have described, and cauterize that with a cautery in the posterior part of the head in the place which is called the vessel — and this is the place where a cupping glass is applied. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient because of this finds much greater pain, different from the pain of all other cauterizations of the head.
And I say that this cautery in its place, because it is necessary that the cautery with which the temples of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
On the cauterization of recent migraine.
When a sharp pain occurs in the head, or a prolonged pain extends to the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the sections on diseases; and if all this does not help, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it, and cut off its extremities from both ends. Then incise the place of the pain in the temple with a lancet, until a space is made under the skin, so that it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress to cause constriction, and leave it for three hours; then unbind it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a cotton ball soaked in vinegar until the area becomes inflamed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner:  heat the cautery, of which this is the shape:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is like the shape of a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle part of it there is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the cauterized area which is underneath is not burned further — and rotate the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount that is burned by the sharpness of the cautery is as much as half of it, raise your hand so that the artery which is underneath is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; and then place cotton soaked in wine on it, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife as with a cautery, and be careful not to cut an artery in this particular, not-ancient, migraine.
Chapter four.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because of the illness what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is insufficient, then you should heat the knife-cautery until it is white-hot, then mark the location of the pain with a line whose length is half the length of a finger, or something like that; and place your hand for a moment and press until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle is not injured and the nerve which moves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the guarding and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, for in its incision there is danger, and especially to him who is ignorant of what he does and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation to him at the beginning.
And I will come with a reminder of the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if sometimes this illness is not alleviated by this cautery and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize that in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of arteries with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatments which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is what is called a puncture, of which this is the form:
And then puncture, then apply the cautery, and let it be moved around the whole ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is in a specific area. Extend the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, after you have marked the area with a probe.
And if the cautery requires ten punctures in the whole ear, or in a part of it, then subsequently treat the areas until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
On the cauterization of a sprain/twist.
A sprain, which is cured with cauterization, is only one of the types that arise from phlegm, as we have said in the classifications of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that occurs due to dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat this type of sprain with leeches, purgatives, and gargles, and your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part, because softening (or alleviation) only comes to the part that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the ear, a little above the corner of the head, and another at the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger, and keep your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of the  fingernail is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the shape of which is shown above/previously. However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some superfluous thickness.
And then care for the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you have treated it with what we have said, and your medication is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize them with four cauteries: on all the corners of their head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the nape of the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is also cauterized above the stomach; and it is better behind; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness done by flame.
So that large enemas and purifying pills are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply to their neck a plaster of cetre described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cautery. And do this according to the same order which I have already mentioned.
And therefore if he is healed by these means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauteries on the back of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been prescribed.
But if you add to it and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize in the middle of the place; and if you wish a greater addition, then cauterize that upon the two horns (presumably referring to two points flanking a central point).
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
That you proceed in the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery over all the horns (presumably points) of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries over the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to treat it, and the patient is tolerant and the illness is severe, then cauterize with four cauteries over the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from the tailbone, and raise your hand. And then care for it until it is healed, and this should be an olive oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
On the cauterization of epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and tolerant of the expulsion of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, beforehand for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the patient's head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the occiput, and cauterize on each temple. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let it be an olive-shaped cautery as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small/fine cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has been described previously in the division (of treatments).
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a decent-sized poultice for him on a circular cloth, and then place that in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held securely on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is most worthy to use old sheep's fat (or tallow). And heat that (liniment) over the fire with moderate warmth, which the said sufferer will feel when it is applied to him. Then pour that (liniment) into the middle of his head within the circle, and let it remain there until it is cold.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other treatment, until he is healed; or, if you wish, cauterize that (area) making small punctures according to his need, provided that you hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a type of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in sheep's fat or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the onset of water in the eye is apparent to you, through the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to drink which will purify his head, and protect it from all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath of some kind for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and also cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the onset of the flow of water is in both eyes, or on one side if the onset of the water is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the hairline; and let the cauterizations be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with any medicine which is available to you.
And I come with skill which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of these and the control of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed far apart.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (or, Concerning the cauterization of chronic fistulas/ulcers).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location on the outside of the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the beginning chapter concerning water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it needs additional treatment, then cauterize it at the end of the eyebrow above the outer canthus of the eye with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or, Concerning cauterization for a foul-smelling nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient the decoctions for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize it with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauteries above the two eyebrows, a little below the hair.
And beware of the artery, that you do not burn it.
And this is the shape of the clove-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the cauterization for the softening of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that, do so a little above the two eyebrows with two cauteries on each side, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too hard with the cautery, but only enough that a third part of the eyelid is dried/cauterized.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in those cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either with fire or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize it with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave his eyelashes like those who cleanse them, until they are elongated and even; and if they prick from their roots, then bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not irritated until the hairs have grown back.
Then, when they are grown back and even, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with a probe, with a mark that is in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then apply cotton soaked in egg white or in a mucilage of ptils (likely referring to a type of plant) over the eye.
And then ignite the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the marked figure, little by little, in several times, until the surface of the skin, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and its hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it like that for three days, and then apply cotton with a bandage until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their root and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are below the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is even, and the hairs do not touch the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; this is that you instruct the patient to pull the hairs until they are elongated and even, and then a myrtle leaf-shaped piece of linen is applied.
Then take known soap and quicklime, about one ounce, or thereabouts, and mix these ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; and then spread this on the linen which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place this over one or both eyes; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it slightly, but do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like that of fire.
And when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and move it with your finger; when the bite ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider whether you see that the eyelid is now raised as if it were cauterized with fire or with incision, for then it is well; and if not, return some of the medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet reached and it does not blacken until your operation is even and the eye is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab from the cauterization is removed, and then treat it with healing ointment or with something else until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to guard with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid is softened after a few days and some of the hairs pierce the eye, then repeat your medicine on the place where the eyelid is softened, precisely as you did at the beginning, and then cure it until it is healed.
And this is the method for myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and therefore your cauterization is done accordingly.
And the right way will not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the lacrimal duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not successful, then you should cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place his head on your lap, and have a minister hold his head between your hands in such a way that his head is not moved or agitated.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or gum arabic on his eye.
Then heat the cautery, which is shaped like this:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you want, let it be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from it.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, upon it; and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand may not wander or the patient be disturbed. Then place the cautery on the granulation of the eye and burn it away.
And so, if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it thus for three days, and then place upon it cotton with a bandage and surround it with drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then place a sharp, corrosive medicine upon it until the bone is uncovered and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier physicians described:  Attend to the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a fine, thin tube, which is shaped like this:  And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of melted lead; and hold your hand with a clamp with good retention, and let not the patient be moved so that the melted lead does not reach his eye; and it is necessary that you place upon the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will adhere to the location of the fistula and heal it by a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a probe through the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
On the cauterization of the fissure of the wells (likely referring to fistulas or similar openings).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the divisions and your treatment does not help, then apply a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that instrument in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
And then treat that with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and then you treat it and the treatment is not effective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula. Then place this heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, holding your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it, and then do this once or twice, afterwards; and then treat that with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And so, if the wound is excised and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to expose the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be described in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by humidity and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and they do not help, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be described below, in such a way that you place the small spatula on the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization a few times as needed.
And then let the patient rinse their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupted humidity is dried out.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or a worm in it, and you do not obtain a cure with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a cow's horn, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a horn is that you take a cow's horn and boil it in a pan, and then take cotton and wrap it around the tip of a probe, and then dip it in the boiling horn, and place it on the aching tooth quickly until it is cooled.
And then repeat this several times until the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned boiling horn, and place that on the aching tooth, and place a glowing iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you take a small rod of brass or iron, and that there is some thickness in its middle so the sharpness of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired shape, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be relieved either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, by the application of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good broth and hold it for an hour, then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophulos.  (Extrophulos likely refers to some kind of external growth or lesion.)
When extrophulos are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not amenable to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them mildly, then ignite the cautery, the shape of which is as follows: having two open ends so that smoke escapes from one end of the cautery, and place this ignited instrument on that abscess once, and again if it is needed, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in wine over it until what the fire has burned is healed.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and constriction of the throat.
When humidities come upon the lung passage, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and afterwards cauterize below the root of the throat in the depressed area.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn his windpipe, only a little.
And cauterize that with another cautery near the juncture of the neck at his last vertebra. And his cautery should be a nail, following the previous manner.
And afterwards, treat that with what has been previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has neither fever nor consumption [tuberculosis], and the illness is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle in the previously mentioned places, left and right; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the previous form.
And if you wish, let your cauterization be punctate with the cautery that is called a punctate cautery, the form of which precedes in the punctation of earache.
And let the points be close to those; and then treat it with the same things that were previously prescribed until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs, for cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this form:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the titillic, which is the head of the penis.
When the head of the penis is dislocated due to lubricous humors and is not fixed in the time of its return from its dislocation until that which is accustomed to returning is located there, and then by a small movement it is dislocated again as it should be, as we have testified, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his side; and then elevate the glans which is in the inner part of the penis to the outer parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated to the inner parts.
And then heat the cautery having two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the glans until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place on the cautery cooked pork with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the humidity is removed and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors enter the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying within it, and if you continue the methods of cure and do not succeed, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his arms and legs, and then cauterize him with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, the length of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauteries below on either side of the cautery, so that a triangular figure of cauteries is formed; and space them so that they do not touch, which would cause rotting.
And if the depth of the cautery is two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the shape of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery according to the form of a circle, according to this form:  (The phrase implies diagrams were present in the original text to show the shapes of the cauteries).
And let the stomach be cauterized, paying attention to what is feared from this cauterization: and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the quantity you wish with a probe, and then cauterize these points with the cautery; and then treat it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
Of the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is expelled from its normal state by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it doesn't help, then it is necessary that the patient lie supine on their back, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity on the liver under the hypochondria, where the recumbent position of a man allows. (Again, implies accompanying diagrams)
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of the right index finger.
And do not press too hard with the cautery.
And if the quantity of what is burned is the quantity of half the thickness of a finger, and no more; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, having his thighs extended, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-shaped cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to cauterize excessively, and let the thickness of a whole finger be burned so that the entire body is burned and reaches the intestines, because the finger here is thin; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the perforation of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When a wound or abscess develops in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient experiences heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, the pain is very sharp and sometimes it fatigues the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be positioned on their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then ignite the cautery, which is shaped like a rake, the form of which was described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the pus is consumed, until it reaches the bottom, until healthy matter emerges.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this form of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many illnesses have been treated; but let someone with some experience sometimes dare this operation, however, leave it to us, and it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, according to this manner, is effective, and it is this: that you take a long, dry root of birthwort, one finger in length, which is found, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in that place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly leaning towards the part which is below the beard, and with two cauteries in that which is leaning between the fifth and sixth ribs, leaning slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another above the stomach, and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in that place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp your hand with the cautery, but rather let it be in the manifest action of the handle.
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray, and ignite it, and put it in that place which is between the ribs, until it reaches that abscess and is thoroughly healed, as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But in this perforation with cautery there is danger, because you might kill him immediately in the fistula site, to which there is no healing.
Chapter 30.
Of the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said about treatment in the division and you do not succeed in your treatment, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all of which are correct. One of these is to make three or four cauteries lengthwise, according to the length of the spleen, following the pattern of the liver cauteries, the form of which has been described previously.
And let the width between each cautery be that of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we have described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not compress with your hand over the cautery; and let the patient be lying on their side.
And another way of cauterization is to ignite the cautery having two prongs, which method we have described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the rib which is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And let your elevation, with which you raise the rib, follow the length of the body, so that cauteries are made according to the width of the body.
And then insert the two prongs, very hot, into that space so that with them you penetrate the rib from the other side.
And then extract the cautery; and thus four cauteries are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three prongs.
And then you treat the cauterization once it has stopped oozing pus for several days, for this is more important than the other preceding actions in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical patient.
Cauterization is not used in dropsy except in a certain specific circumstance.  (The "3" after alcinte is likely a scribal error or annotation.)
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we have described in the division and your treatment is ineffective, then it is necessary that you cauterize them with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cautery above the stomach, and with another cautery above the liver, and with another cautery above the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery above the stomach. And the depth of the cauterization should be proportionate to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cauterization open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment near the cauterization with what is necessary, so that two intentions are added to it, and healing is hastened with assistance. (This sentence is somewhat ambiguous and could benefit from further context to be fully understood. The "two intentions" likely refers to two therapeutic aims.)
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its form is described above.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed with dropsy and filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the dorsum of the foot, in the space between the little and ring fingers. Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand again, and do not completely withdraw it, because the yellowish water will flow out.
The cautery should be of this shape: Then make two cauterizations on the two thighs, and on each thigh, let the cauterization be done with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh; one below the knee and the other in the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh, two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is afflicted by cold and humidity which weakens its functions – that is, the digestive and retentive functions which are located in the stomach and intestines – and this is treated with methods of healing and does not improve, and sometimes the patient suffers, exhibiting a need for cauterization, then cauterize that area with a large cautery over the stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries in a circle around the navel, with a thin, pointed cautery; and with cautery on the hypochondrium, above the large intestine, or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize the area over the pubic bone and over the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries over the stomach and near the inguinal regions, for this is a perfect cure and a helpful treatment, in which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are chronic hemorrhoids, or those caused by thick cold humors or corrupted humors, and they are treated with what we have mentioned in the division and your treatment does not help, then cauterize with three cauteries below the lowest vertebra of the sacrum, small and triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, near two fingers breadth.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on the spleen, using a nail-shaped cautery; and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of athel and their excision.  (Note: "athel" likely refers to a type of growth or swelling; the precise meaning would require further context within the medical treatise.)
When you excise athel, then immediately apply a cautery that resembles a ray. (Again, "athel" is unclear without more context, but it seems to refer to a growth or lesion.) Then insert that ignited cautery into the excised athel, and finally place your hand there until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows.
And do this once or twice, for if the athel are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat them with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is more difficult to heal.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the cauterization of fistulas which are formed unnaturally in the parts of the body.
When nature does not obey the treatment we have recommended, and the patient is distressed by this, let it be treated with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in an unnatural location, and it is old, with pus and corrupted humidity, then what is most fitting is to probe it with a thin probe, and then ascertain the extent of its depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery iron, which resembles a ray (or rod). Then insert that ignited cautery into the fistula, according to the depth indicated by the probe.
And repeatedly apply the cautery until those corrupted parts are burnt, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And beware of burning nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or the intestine, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you believe it is not penetrating (deeper vital structures).
Then care for the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the materials have been excised from it, and it remains so for a long time, then know that it is completely healed.
And if the materials have not been removed, then know that it is penetrating or the bone underneath is corrupted, or this comes from those causes which will be mentioned in their place (later in the text).
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of ulcers.
When in ulcers pain arises from cold or excessive windiness, and because of this the patient's flesh is diminished, then it is necessary that you cauterize that above the ulcers with a cautery on each ulcer with a nail-shaped cautery, the details of which were previously mentioned.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauteries result, and then they will be more helpful in healing.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubis begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped one, as is appropriate/customary.
Chapter 39.
On the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and humidity come to the breast, and because of this conception is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs at the time of their arrival, then it is necessary that the place be cauterized with three cauteries in the circumference of the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery above the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it protrudes.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the area as it is swollen, so that the area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind previously described in the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize that area with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be made according to the amount of the swelling of the entire hip; and then care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and do not disagree, and this is ancient practice, then it is necessary that you purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or with artificial or similar pills; and then cauterize that area.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Now, according to two known methods, one of which is that you cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space between each cautery be the size of a finger's breadth, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the area, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of hip dislocation, that is, let it be a continuous circle and burn the thickness of the entire hip.
And this is the method of cauterization for sciatica when the pain is confined to the hip and does not extend to the thigh.
But if it extends to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place where the patient indicates the pain; and with one cautery on the groin, four fingers slightly below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip.
And if the patient indicates that the pain extends to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not go so deep with the cautery as to burn nerves or large arteries, lest this cause the patient harm, such as numbness or contraction.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg swelled until the tumor reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong bowel movement occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places simultaneously, cauterize each according to this manner.
And the wise ones say that in the cauterization of the ankle, the cautery should be like this: make a circle of iron whose circumference is half a palm; and let its opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a walnut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those rings be one or two nuts, and make in those margins of iron decently prepared in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the ankle, the patient lying on their side. And then cauterize it with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave it for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for several days; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: O, son, of this type of cautery we have used little because of the fear of its appearance, and because we have found few who look upon it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with it the place of it.
But cauterization with burning medicines is that you make two circles having a perforated handle in which the iron tool fits; let the length of the walls in the height of them be two nuts or whatever they are.
And if the space which is between each one is the quantity of the thickness of the finger; and they are open below and above, and they are such that they contain themselves between them according to this form:
Then place it over the part of the anus, the patient lying on their side, and press your hand on the part of the anus; then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and the quality of that should be with small fire little by little, and hold that for the amount of one hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning of it — for that produces a burning like fire — until the biting ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the whole anus with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and place a poultice on it until the blackness of the burn is softened, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final cure.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make for him a bath that contains water, as was done for the anus similarly.
And this is the manner of warm water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, an equal part of each, and crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated by a small hole, according to the quantity that will enter the rectum.
And place under the bottom of the pot another inverted pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime fresh water which covers them by a finger's breadth, and after you compress them with your hand by good compression, and let the sharp water descend from it into the inverted pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of very strong acidity, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterization of other members, because it acts like fire.
And some medicines with which it is also cauterized are, for example,  thrice-boiled honey and quicklime with soap, mixed together.
And Galen recounted the operation and cure of a pain in the hip and sciatic vein from an ancient man, and greatly praised the effectiveness of this remedy, saying that it needed no other cure, because he was healed at once as he recounted, for he quickly entered a bath and immediately was free of it.
And it is that fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be pounded very well with a little fat and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and it should be bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels a lessening of the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that it is not fitting that anyone use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But Dioscorides says that when the varicose vein is cauterized with a goat's hoof, it benefits him greatly; and the cautery should be done in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and the ankle.
Then take a dry goat's hoof and burn it in the fire until it is made into charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it there until it is extinguished. And then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of burning reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this manner of cautery is called cautery of hooves.

Of the cauterization of toothache.

And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
Concerning cauterization at the beginning of a gibbosity (or hump).
Often this illness happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs on their frame in the wing (possibly referring to the shoulder blade or scapula) with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And therefore when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize it with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in all parts of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery, two or three lines. And let the points be close together.
And then care for the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered to a swelling caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is customarily called gout by physicians.
And therefore, if gout caused by cold [humors] is treated with a method of cure and the pains are not relieved, then cautery relieves them.
And it is so that you cauterize that area for evacuation in the circumference of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains ascend to the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize and do not press too hard with your hand with the cautery beforehand; let it only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains ascend to both knees, then administer the cautery which we have mentioned in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called "suagra" (likely a type of hand ailment), then puncture in the circle of the veins as they intertwine; puncture in those two lines.
And if the pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the circle of the veins on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains rise to the hips or muscles in a few days, then cauterize them in every part of the affected areas.
And do not deprive the patient of good regimen and the reception of medicines, for if good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, pushing the intestines in front of the testicle, and this is beginning to happen, then you should order the patient to stop eating that day and to use soothing remedies so that the tear in their abdomen subsides.
Then hold the patient between your hands over his back, and instruct him to hold his breath until the testicle or the intestine is expelled or appears.
Then return that [the intestine/hernia] with your fingers and gently push the rupture against the pubic bone, noting that the extremity should resemble a half-circle in the superior parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is as follows:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and another servant should be at their legs, and another at their feet, who should hold their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach the bone of the pubis in the middle of the perineum; and repeat this another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And carefully observe the protrusion of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm befall the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not successful.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small, according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice, until the burn scar is healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and the wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not protrude due to the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be moderate in work, in the fullness of food and drink, and in loud voices, for when he uses this entire regimen of healing, he will be completely healed.
And I will tell you the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the abdomen; and if, at the beginning, you want them to stop bleeding, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let what is excised from the wound be like a third of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is appropriate.
And some earlier authors said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this shape, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed through the width at the highest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a pointed cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion.
When pain develops in some member due to a fall or percussion, and that pain persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be appropriate to each member according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cautery, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains to be punctuated from one member to the next nearest member, so that it is necessary to treat those with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes treated with cautery with great benefit, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you want to cauterize a leper, consider if the leprosy is spreading and is not cured by what we have said in the division, and we fear that the corruption will spread throughout the patient's body and complexion. Then cauterize the patient's head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second below that, towards the forehead at the hairline; and two on either side of the temples; and one behind, on the nape of the neck or the cervicals.
And finally, with the cautery, press gently so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the crusts are loosened, so that the exhalation of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize on the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already spread over the patient and manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned for the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the coccyx, and one on the coccyx, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the knees and one on each knee, and with two on the ankles, and with two on the muscles of the calves and two on the tendons of the feet; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of the feet, and on the knuckles of the hands.
And be careful of the nerves that are on the heel, so as not to burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do with wounds, because their body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization of stupor (or numbness).
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes a numbness affecting the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back, is cauterized near the emergence of the nerves which supply that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the course of the nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.
When albaras is chronic and you apply none of the medicinal ointments to it, then cauterize it with a cautery iron slightly, according to the size and nature of the lesion, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and arugula leaves, and with pigeon blood or hen blood, of each an equal part and all mixed together, and then spread it on a cloth and apply it to the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is spread.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery iron in the middle; but this is not my practice, for I fear that it will become ulcerated, and I have seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the cauterization of a boil.
When a boil is slow to mature, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or to the age of the patient—because they are old and decrepit, with little blood—or due to the season, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the boil; then cauterize around its circumference with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the boil until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place where there is pus, so that the body of the boil is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On the cauterization of corrosives.
A corrosive is a corruption that travels in the limb, which corrodes it as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that can tolerate cautery with fire, then you should apply many small, or large, hot cautery irons, according to what is appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it entirely until you eradicate all the corrosion and none of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply crushed sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until the scabs are all gone and all the corruption is removed.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And sometimes a corrosive is medicated with a sharp medicine, for that acts in place of cautery. But however, cautery with fire is of quicker help.
And I have already described the treatment of it with sharp medicine in the section, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times, a disease called a wound happens in the parts below the foot, and it is a large, penetrating wound which injures the foot; and cauterization in these cases is according to two methods, that is cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is that you heat a concave cautery similar to a vulture's beak, which is made of iron according to the size which contains the entire wound, and it should be of fine edges.
