And then place that heated instrument on the wound, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed pus. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not heal, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should apply cautery with water to those areas.  Take a small tube of bronze or iron or a vulture's quill, and place it over the wound; then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube, and hold your hand, and move it with the compression of your whole hand, and press the edge of the tube into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the bottom of the wound; and let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for a short time, about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this to those [wounds] and then to the others until you have treated all of those that are in the body.
And then treat those sores, once the roots of the wounds are eradicated, with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremor.
When someone experiences tremor due to cold in the nerves, or due to a fever of a cold origin, or due to a similar cause, then you should cauterize him with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cautery; and with a cautery on his feet, and on his stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And his tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental whiteness in the body.
When in the body there appears whiteness, and there are pustules and fissures which are made of thick, corrupted matter; then at the beginning, when it appears, it should be cauterized on the head of the fistula with a thin cautery with myrtle wood, the tip of which is extended with fire; or with a long root of erestologia; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes cauterization is also suitable at the beginning of its expulsion according to this manner; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is disrupted by doing this, and heals from those.
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of a blood flow accident due to an arrow wound.
Often, there is a flow of blood from an arrow wound that is incised near the wound, which flows outwardly, or from the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand on the orifice of the arrow wound, and place your finger over it tightly many times until the flow of blood stops under your finger, and it does not come out.
Then, put small and large olive cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken, on that vein quickly after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until that blood is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the orifice of the artery when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from those which are prepared in the fire, and do not cease to do this one after the other until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it in those cases, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted, the extremities are better cauterized and the blood is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to stop blood be placed on it; and they are tightened with a cushion with a decent constriction.
But he who administers a decent restriction with a ligature and with constriction with cloths or by the application of burning substances and similar things, does not do so quickly.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently place the index finger on the orifice of that vein as we have said, and apply great pressure to it until the blood is stopped from the main part of the wound, and the finger should not be moved from the artery; and over the artery let cold water of very great coldness be sprinkled until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then consider what need there is of cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire is suitable and with burning medicine, and the occasions and causes thereof, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the top of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one who seeks this will be relieved, then on that which he will have asked.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is what is in the first chapter on cauterization, but for terror. (or "...but in a frightening way").
And because it is necessary that there be great caution in it, for in the operation which is in this chapter many times there is an evacuation which is with blood with which life remains, because of the opening of a vein or incision on the abscess or opening of an exit or purification of the wound or extraction of an arrow or incision of a stone and their like. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in the worst of them, death.
And I warn you that this hesitation should not fall upon you, for sometimes you will encounter men with many kinds of diseases. And of them there is someone who is angry with his illness and death is light for him, and this because of the vehemence of what he finds in his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is fear which signifies death.
And of those there is one who gives you his wealth and says, you hoping for his health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to that which comes to you of which this manner is; and let your caution be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume any cause about this except through certain knowledge which verifies this for you, and to which follows praiseworthy success.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that is to you help towards acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you His understanding and not hinder your direction and protection, for this is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be alleviated for you.
Chapter One.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth and when the nurse compresses the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is not known.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is in my view a fatal condition in them.
And I have seen that a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either accumulated between the skin and the bone, or it is accumulated under the bone on the scalp.
Because if in this case the humidity is between the skin and the bone and the abscess is small, then you must make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and let the length of the fissure be about two nuts so that the humidity can drain, and this is the form of a spatula:
And therefore, if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there should be two incisions in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inner parts, and this is not hidden from you. You must make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then bind the incisions with cloths and lint; and then swab it with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appears, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that flow of blood with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter Second.  (or Chapter Two)
When someone develops cataracts or rheum in the eyes or feet, and this is chronic, and medicinal cures do not work, then the final cure for this is incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, have the patient shave their head, and then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then constrict the patient's neck with a cloth; and then consider where the vein pulses; and the location of each [artery] in the areas near the ears is compressed, and thus they are hidden except in some men.
And then mark the artery with an ink pen, and then incise each one with the sharp point of a scalpel, making an incision reaching to the bone, and this should be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth, because when a vein is incised, the blood exits with a pulsating flow, which spurts out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers' width, and then mark it with ink, and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be let is about six ounces, and quickly let more or less, and all this is according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any fragment of the splinter remains on the bone, then excise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
Then bind the wound tightly with lint and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrid humors and a diseased quality in the tissues of the temples, or a severe and similar chronic condition, and treatment with medicinal methods fails, then in these illnesses we should attempt extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization, as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and attend to the artery appearing in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden only in few men by the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with warm water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then put in it [the artery] a hook and draw it to the upper parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the hook, and then incise a part of it according to the quantity by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they are not incised, the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at length, and do not let it putrefy before the consolidation of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And then incise the excess which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of that same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot complexion; because cautery is one of those things that helps to dry out excessive humors, and therefore it is better in this case.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you clean the wound with old linen, and that decently made powders are placed on it, and then it should be treated with drying medicines that cause the flesh to grow and with bandages until it is healed.
And if, while you operate another time, there is a flow of blood from the artery, then you must take care to restrain that blood, either with cautery, or by filling the place with linen, and squeezing your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things are at hand, then place your finger on the area until the blood is congealed, and apply water of intense coldness to the area until the sharpness subsides and it is constricted as needed.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each, very hot, until they reach the bone and incise the vein in two places, so that that which is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. But however, if it has less sharpness than two knives it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives close to each other, it will not incise the flesh with speed.
Therefore, since in those there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is incised lightly.
And this method is better in every way and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two blades be the quantity of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
On the treatment of sharp and acrid eye discharges.
When there are acrid tears, and the source is those veins which are on the scalp, and you find no cause for treatment in medicine, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and feels a crawling sensation on the forehead as if it were ants crawling, and their eyes are swollen, moist, so that the hairs around them are stuck together, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridity of the tears, the ancients used to employ in the treatment of these, this operation, and they called this treatment "spassa".
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on his forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure reaches two fingers: one fissure should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length, a little above the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal, and the third on the other side.
And beware of the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three fingers adjusted (or, ...the quantity of three finger-widths).
And have ready a piece of sponge or dried cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that (the spatula) all the skin which is between the two incisions with the scalpel which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that (curette) against the flesh attached to the skin, and its blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that appearance of the skin, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
Then place in each fissure a pledget of old linen, and place upon them powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of tears from cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone suffers from sharp and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes are noticeably weakened and small, and their vision is impaired, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their hairs fall out, and there is acute, throbbing pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the matter and cataracts come from the many veins originating in the inner parts.
And the best cure in such cases is this: that you instruct the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be between the bones; and extend your hand from the temples' lacerations which are softened by chewing), until the bone is uncovered.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders soaked in wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then it is necessary to scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow there, and then treat it with a drying regimen which causes the flesh to grow, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and make a plaster from them and apply it to these wounds to promote the growth of flesh.
Chapter 6.
On the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Every cause that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass, or it is from a plant such as a thistle or bean, or it is a flowing substance such as water and sour wine and things like them, or some animal.
And therefore, when a stone falls into the ear or another similar cause from among those things which do not adhere to or grow in the ear, and expose the ear to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then proceed with its extraction, and induce sneezing with a potion, and block the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards, for many times it is expelled with these measures.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a fine ear pick, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the pick, it is good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction with a thin probe of little doubling (or slight curvature).
And therefore, if it is not expelled by this, then make a copper cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the opening of the ear and seal around the cannula with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the cannula. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times it happens as we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small quantity of lit tow or bird-lime prepared for catching birds, and place it on the end of a ray (possibly referring to a thin rod or sliver of wood), and then carefully wrap it around that cotton. And then gently insert that into the opening of the ear, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the means we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to their strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear below its cartilage in the well-prepared area. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until the stone is reached; and, the stone found, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; and then close the incision immediately, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause lodged in the ear is a growth that is growing and swollen, then administer its treatment and extraction; but if it does not obey such treatment, then take finely ground alum according to this method: and administer with that the removal of the growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on small pieces of cloth, and then extract them with tweezers or with a fine instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus facilitated.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing as a first measure, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take several heated stones or pebbles, about the length of a finger, and heat them slightly in the fire, and the patient inserts one of these into the opening of his ear and jumps on one foot on that side and strikes with another stone upon the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or tow, and place one end in the ear and hold the other end to the fire until the majority of it is burnt.
And then put in more tow and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and extract it with a cannula as described above for the stone extraction.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a fly or something similar, cure it as we have said in the division; but if it is a large creature, which appears to the sensible judgment, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and the cause of it is lighter than all the other causes that befall the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you cure it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your cure does not succeed, then you should expose the ear to the sun.
And so if some cause of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks, but if no cause appears, then take a cannula narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And introduce the thin end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it strongly, and do this many times until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you fill and close the circumference of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the cure of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and wide above.
And so be it that they push what is in the cavity of the container cannula where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray (possibly referring to a type of plant stem or reed) and wrap its extremity decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the cannula—and that is in the ear—and then introduce the ray with cotton on top and press with your hand that with a moderate compression until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And that which is poured into the ear should already be warmed a little with fire. And be careful that the substance which you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children are born from their mothers' wombs without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal by a wound or by excess flesh in it; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the preauricular area, which does not allow drainage, and sometimes in the outer parts.
And that which occurs in the preauricular area, which does not allow drainage, is in the most difficult conditions for healing.
And in that which allows drainage, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. Then, if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its extremity is small in width, one side being sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two finger-widths wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its meatus, then take that with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the earlobe, then take a fine, thin probe and warm it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
Then, if you have felt an obstruction with which the patient experiences difficulty, due to the severity of hearing,  remove the obstruction gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a piece of linen according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some lint and soak it in rosewater and sprinkle on it finely ground  [likely a healing powder - the specific ingredient is unclear], and use it; and if you see the ear is already abscessed, then you must remove the lint and replace it with other lint which you will wrap in a plaster made with rose oil, until the abscess is fully reduced; and then cure it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which constricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
On the treatment of a stye which occurs on the eyelids.
Styes which occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with fingers, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then apply a poultice to the affected areas.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a fine, lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there develops in the eyelids of the eyes a certain condition which resembles swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of this swelling it is called [a swelling/tumor].
And it is an aggregation of thick humidity in the eyelid below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling proceeds from that which appears on the eyelid and is spread to every part; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on that with a lancet in width, and then peel it away from every part with ease until it comes off, and then lift it up with tweezers and cut it off.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, it will not harm you because its ointment restores it and causes it to heal.
And if the growth extends to the inner parts of the eyelid beyond the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and lift the growth with tweezers,  even though you need to make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by incision, this harms nothing.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with something that heals until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a boil on the eyelid above the eye.
A stye is a swelling which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and especially in children; it inflames their eyes and makes them sensitive to light; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears flow from them.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their lower eyelids, below the eyebrows, are moist, already covered with inflammation, and the imminence of a stye is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel a stye within.
And a method of operation is this: the patient rests his head on your lap, and you place a cloth on him and make him a poultice, and make for him a circle according to the size which contains the stye entirely, and then place this on the stye and press with your fingers on all sides so that the stye is pressed into the middle of the circle, and then make in the middle of the moistness with a lancet an incision with a fissure, and the fissure should not be larger than the fissure that is made in phlebotomy.
But in the beginning it is necessary that you cut the skin completely until you reach the stye, for in most cases the stye proceeds by fissure according to the form of a white swelling.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers on the outside parts and roll it between your right and left hand until it is gone.
And be careful not to push into the fissure, lest with the lancet you reach the eye and injure it.
But if the stye does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then push gently into the incision a little until the stye appears; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar and place them over the area and bind them with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the fissure so that it dries up the remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with emollient plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the ways of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural hairs and are inflamed, then these harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membranes, until this becomes the cause of the destruction of the eye, which is called tactus (a touching or inflammation) of the eye, and it is cured in four ways:  either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery; or the tactus is treated by incision and suture; or by cannulas, as I shall explain.
And therefore it is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap; then evert the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is everted, good; and if not, introduce a needle into that part where there is pus under the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate upward, and this should be — near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow — near the hairs, and draw the pus to the upper parts with the eyelid, and evert it with a ray (of light, perhaps referring to using light to see better).
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid, beneath the inflamed hairs, with a pointed instrument, from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller; and then extract the pus and place small pieces of cotton or linen under the eyelid, and then bind it over the eyelid with a bandage, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of elevation of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for there are those who require a great deal of incision of the eyelid according to the extent to which the eyelid is softened.
And then make an incision with the pointed instrument along the two lines which you have marked, beginning from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, let it be the thickness of a king's hair (i.e., a very fine incision).
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it completely, and then join the two lips with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick any excess glue onto two eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
And then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it (the wound).
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its closing; but suturing is better. And therefore the ancients advised this.
However, let there be diligence in the patient's care, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear in it.
And there is another method of treatment, which is to mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three hooks separated and joined according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the hooks, and this is not equal, then take a needle on which there is thread and place that in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger lacrimal duct, and the second thread near the smaller lacrimal duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with those, with equal elevation, raise the whole eyelid on which is marked as we have recounted.
And then close the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use on that what we have said of incision; but suture in each case is more skillful.
Bandaging with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and incise the growth that is within as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two thin canes or splints whose length is according to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add each one over what is superfluous of the eyelid's skin, and bind the canes to each part with a firm binding, and leave it for some days, for the growth, because of the binding, will die and dry out and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then cure the wound until it is healed.
Then, when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the skin does not prick.
And this is the form of two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is stiff.
When unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs are in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle and place on it a fine silk or linen thread, and then adjust its ends so that you tie each one in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be about the length of a palm.
And then you prepare another short, fine thread underneath, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
Then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which requires skill, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it from above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair upwards and insert it into the alixati (likely a type of tweezers or forceps), and let there be one, two, or three hairs, but no more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair is pulled out with the alixolati (tweezers/forceps) from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if the alixalati (tweezers/forceps) is pulled and the hair is not removed, you will pull the alixalati downwards with the thread which you have composed with it, until the alixalati is removed from the wound above.
And examine it with the vision of your eye, for then you will remove the alixalati and the thread, and clean each and close the wound, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened to a certain space, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the treatment of an ehacratir (likely a type of stye or eyelid inflammation) which appears on the upper eyelid.
When an ehacratir appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is drawn upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called lagophthalmos (leporina likely means hare-lip like, referring to the appearance), and this ehacratir is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, or by something similar which has been poorly treated.
The method of operation is that you incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do this, the ehacratir will return worse than it was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice with the water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or diachylon ointment which has been liquefied with some oil, and soak the linen cloth in it, and treat it with this.
And the key to your treatment is that you prevent it from consolidating improperly, in a way different from its initial form.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing but a correction of the ehalixacrati (likely a misspelling or archaic term for a medical condition) that does not return the area to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of ehacrati (likely a misspelling or archaic term for a medical condition) which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This *alxacrati* (likely a variant spelling of the previous term), which occurs in the lower eyelid—which is truly called *alxacrati*—is sometimes natural, sometimes accidental.
But accidental *alxacrati* is caused by a wound, or by a cut, or by cautery, or by other similar things.
And the method of its operation is that you take a needle with double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh (the affected area of the eyelid).
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And then if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, it is good; but if not, take a splint and place it in the divided area, and with it restore the eyelid, and close the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be at two angles of the incision which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be made of them so that when they come together, their shape is formed.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
Then remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp lower eyelid is, so that it becomes the eyelid.
Then join the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is excised by fissure, suture, or cautery, then you must make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid as previously described. Then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips as we have said.
In summary, concerning the treatment of an abscess whether above or below, let the operation proceed as prepared in the manner of an abscess, and this is in the form of many varieties.
And the skillful surgeon always prepares instruments in every way possible so that he may restore the form to its natural state, or nearly so, and the patient, according to what suits him in the operation and the instruments, in the best possible condition.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in many men such that the upper eyelid is solidified with the white of the eye, and is prevented from its free movement and natural functions, then it is necessary that you insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid and elevate it, or spread it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision carefully separate that adhesion easily until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the whole adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it some  henbane dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small linen cloth, and place on the eye wool infused in egg white, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two kinds, for it is either nervous, and is similar to hard, fine parchment; or it is non-nervous, similar to congealed white humidity, which when an iron touches it, or it is forced to be removed with an instrument, is severed and the instrument is not inserted into it.
And both types begin only from the larger tear duct until it gradually covers the sight and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
But the method of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head on your lap, and then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with an instrument of little thickness and extend it to the parts above. And then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the tip of the needle little by little and place it in the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the parts above, and scrape with the hair the edge of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair into the back of it.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that by the incision of it there is no continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail, with only the instrument extended or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is its form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then the incision, put in the eye a little crushed salt or a poultice made of flax, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is the instrument inserted into it, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the patient's eye and take a thin lion spatula of iron sharpened according to this form: with subtlety.  (or, with precision/delicacy) And if you see that it is rarefied and is hidden and trapped in that root, then distill from your hour into the eye a poultice made of flax or with rue or with crushed salt, and occlude the eye until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is there is removed, unless the contrary happens and a hot abscess forms in the eye, for then you leave that, and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed.
The prominence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticed in the eye as a great harm, should be suspended with an oncia (a unit of weight, implying a small weight or possibly a surgical instrument); and incise it in part, and do not extend the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
And then instill into the lachrymal duct a poultice of rue and flaxseed until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a staphyloma and that which is born of fleshy matter in the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born and grows until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses upon the eyelids, and strongly compresses the eyelids on the outer parts, and resembles the rose of a balustrade (or, a rose-like growth).
But the operation is that the patient places his head on your lap; and then open the patient's eyes and collect all that flesh with instruments suitable for this, and hold it with forceps or with scissors; and then incise the flesh from the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and be careful of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision, with which you incise those [fleshy growths], be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which the scab is collected, according to the form which will come to it in this chapter.
Then, when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it a red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a bandage over the eye with egg white until it is assured of a hot abscess.
And if any cause of a fleshy growth remains and hot blood is drawn, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and cure that abscess with whatever will reduce that abscess, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do so for the fleshy growth that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, you must avoid any treatment of the fragile eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member is able to tolerate that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is caused by inflamed veins which are swollen on the eye and prevent the vision from its function, and they weaken the eye over a long period of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and there is no other illness in it besides the pus; then you may collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an ounce or two, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of subtle duplication according to this form:
or two ounces gradually in one piece according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins be removed from the eye and be retracted.
And beware that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is at midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distil into the eye verdigris or alum so that with its sharpness it corrodes what is removed from the eyelid.
But if that is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is calmed and he is safe from a hot abscess.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Concerning the reduction of nasal dislocations.
When you treat a dislocation with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as described previously, and it is not healed, then do not make incisions in it but rather, incise the abscess above its maturity, and extract all the moisture that is within it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
And so when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a sharp head, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round, like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle pointing (as is in the following form).
And place that [instrument] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the area with drying and styptic substances.
Then if the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown there, and the body of the pus is excised and remains for 40 days, and it is not renewed and is not abscessed and no accident occurs there, then you will know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no other method than to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this manner:  that is, you uncover the second bone with iron or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a file according to this form:
Let the tip of the iron be triangular, and its shaft be thin and tapered to the tip, like this [presumably a diagram was present in the original].
And then place that [the triangular iron instrument] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal [tear duct]; and move your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the file until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel this because they will feel the air escaping when you move your hand.
Then restore the area with styptic, drying medicines as you have been taught.
Then, when the flesh is born again and the area is hardened, the matter which flowed to the outer parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety and there is no accompanying injury, diminution, or impairment of vision, then you should leave the patient alone, and closely observe them.  Apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suction, for good evacuation.  Then apply to the eye an ointment made from  acacia, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla, and firmly secure the plaster over the eye with many powders.
And the method for the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded in four layers; and make many of these.
Then place these one upon the other, according to the amount that the eye requires. Then bind these firmly with a strong, hard cloth; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye tightly bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it should be bound very tightly.
Then remove the bandage day and night, as we have said.
And if you see the eye is already reduced, it is good; but if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders, and the binding, and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.  (Note:  "uvea tunic" refers to the iris and ciliary body.)
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted: you introduce the needle into the root of the uvea from below upwards, and then place another needle with double thread from the lachrymal side, and make it penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the area of the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea in the circumference of the needle, and tighten that firmly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and allow the thread to remain with the uvea.
And then treat the eye with what strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth within the eye a large prominent area, and then it should be perforated with a thin spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and run the albuginea (white of the eye) humour, and lower the eye and it will enter immediately.
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of alconati (likely a disease or condition, the exact meaning requires further context).
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a humour which is located in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that.
But the method of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight chair, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and gently manipulate it until you see the humour descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried so it does not descend further, and light should be avoided, and the patient should see things as he usually sees.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water (referring to the humour).
But if the descent of the humor is not achieved by what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin spatula and use it on the panniculus, carefully scraping a little below the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the limbus (near the empty space), until the humor is expelled.
Then, once it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have previously boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of the water descending from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of water in the division and which ones it is appropriate to puncture, explaining this according to their nature/health condition.
Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands on a stool, and tie his healthy eye, binding it tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his eye, if the eye in which the water is located is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
And then place the tip of the almond near the cornea, at the edge of the iris in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller lachrymal gland, and gently press the almond with a rotating motion.
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space (or cavity).
And it is necessary that the distance from the almond to the pupil be the same as the space between the pupil and the edge of the blackness (iris), which is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with this pupil the vision of the eye through the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water (aqueous humor) is, and then draw it down one time after another, and if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see that upon which his sight rests immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then, if the water has descended, you should then pause again before the almond is ejected.
And then, when it is dried and does not descend, you extract the almond easily, and gently rotate it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and wash the eye inside with it; and then place on the outside of the eye cotton or tow or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time place on that [wound] crushed comfrey with egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness — for there are some men whose eyes are very hard — then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is named alberid, of which this is the form:
Then perforate that joint with it, and do not insert your hand into the hole, but only make a subtle passage for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let a sleeping place be prepared for the patient, decently made, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room, and let all movements be prohibited.
And position him in such a way that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its disposition until the third day.
And then unbind it in that dark room and expose the affected area to view, and show him some things, and then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Furthermore, it is necessary that you avoid this because the water might easily escape due to great and vehement movement.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you unbind the wound before the seventh day, and rectify it with what is suitable for an abscess.
Then you uncover the affected area and place a mask over his face, by which his sight is regulated, keeping him for some days in the dark room.
And then let him be removed from that dark place gradually and conversed with regarding his affairs.
And know that in this situation the student is not excused from some observations, and then you should be bold in the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Al-Andalus, and some say that fire is made in a perforated earthen vessel, by which the water is sucked up.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read this in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is novel.
And these are the forms of the types of earthenware so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And let them be made of bronze or iron properly, and let their extremities be of this triangulated subtlety, the sharp extremity thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
Of the cure of rotten flesh in the nose.
When in the nose there are born diverse fleshy growths, of which some cause is assembled like a very small scorpion, or a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp, for it is similar to that fish in representation. And from it is cancerous flesh, foul, of dark color; and from it is flesh that is not of dark color, a lion. (The last phrase is unclear. "Carn leon" may be a descriptive term, possibly referring to a specific type of growth, or it could be a scribal error).
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths, neither fraudulent nor cancerous, it is fitting that you make the patient sit between your hands and place him in the sun, and open his nostrils, and prick your ounce (likely a unit of weight, perhaps referring to an amount of a specific medicinal substance) into those fleshy growths. And then extract that to the outside parts, and then incise that which you have grasped of those with a sharp, subtle spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; that if some cause remains from it of which the excision is possible, then you scrape that with one of the subtle instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it swells with blood or a hot abscess forms, then you apply to it what is fitting; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you cut and cauterize that until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
And then let there be poured into the nose near the incision, strong wine and water, or wine. But if the nose is opened and humidity proceeds from it into the palate, then know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is flesh embedded in the pus above the bones of the nostrils to which excision has not reached.
Then it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread into it, to which some thickness is attached, and tie on it many knots, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and instruct the patient and introduce one end of the thread into the nostril with a probe or with any other instrument that is possible for you.
And after you have done this, let him draw its scent until it reaches the canals of the nostrils, and it comes out over his throat, for many times children do something similar at school, and it is an easy thing for anyone who wants to do it.
And then place the two ends of the thread, one end that comes out over the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and then administer a piece of meat with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is already with the knots of the thread.
And then extract the thread, and place near the nose, near the extraction of the blood, a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a more intense cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small conch shell, and the channel similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purger instrument should be flat and open; it should have a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever you want of the juices and extractable substances in it.
Chapter 25.
Of boils that grow in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil grows in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days, before much attention is paid to it.
And for that reason, you should incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate it entirely. Then place on the spot either a cautery or a burning medicine which stays in place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, firm, dark in color, and insensitive; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it which he previously did not have.
And if the abscess is slightly swollen and not dark in color, and it seems that incision is possible throughout, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the place with that which dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should have little confidence in that operation except in certain cases.
Therefore, when it happens to someone that they suffer from any of those causes, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then it is necessary that you scrape each part of its apparent skin until blood comes out. Then join the two edges with sutures and tighten them, and on them sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed linseed, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, and then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the stitches are shed, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have recounted in the suture of the abdomen, or close the solution of continuity with thread as has been taught to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which come between the two edges in the inner parts.
Many times there appear in the inner parts of the lips of men certain hard abscesses resembling the fat of a hedgehog, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Then it is necessary that you separate the lips and  incise each of those hardnesses completely, and then fill the place of the excision with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then wash it with sharp wine and treat the place with something styptic until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the incision of the flesh grown in the gums.
Often a growth appears on the gum, which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you lift that with tongs or hold it with forceps and incise it down to its root, and let the serum or blood flow.
And then place crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders on the area.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, sticky scabs are found, and sometimes they are blackened and become yellow or green, so that the gum is affected by this corruption, and the teeth become loose.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and rest their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those scabs appear and similar substances like sand, until nothing of them remains, and do this for the blackness and yellowness and the others until they are cleaned.
Then if that which is at the beginning of the treatment is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on them the next day and the second and the third until you arrive at the desired end.
And know that teeth require many scrapers of diverse forms and shapes, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped on the inside is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms, and these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that compensates for its loss when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, which is an excuse for its extraction.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is diseased, because many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the work of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is diseased, and you know it well, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, beginning little by little until it is completely loose.
Then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or firm grip, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold it well and it does not move, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not removed, take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth on all sides easily and forcefully remove it in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you should fill that crack with a cloth and tighten it with a thin, fine thread so that it is not broken at the time of your traction on it with forceps.
And you must carefully open with scarification around the entire gum and watch your strength so as not to break it and leave a part of it behind, which would return to afflict the patient with a worse condition than the initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity in extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and the roots of their teeth remain, some or all of them, or that they extract the tooth along with some bone from the jaw, as we have often testified.
And after the extraction, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding occurs from this, as often happens, then crush some amount of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if this alum does not work, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and fine tongs with which the tooth is initially grasped should be long in the gripping part, short in the handle, and thick, so that they do not bend when working with them.
And let them be like tongs with a thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they do not bend; the gripping ends should be short.
And let them be made of Indian iron, precisely made with straight ends, and at the ends of these let there be teeth, of which some interlock with others and tighten by strong compression; and sometimes the ends of these should be in the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong compression.
Chapter 31.
On the extraction of teeth and the removal of broken jawbones.
When you must extract a tooth that is broken and some part remains, then you should apply a poultice to the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then introduce pliers or forceps, the ends of which are shaped like the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Let their ends be made inwardly like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you easily with these forceps or pliers, then you should dig around the root and remove all the flesh and introduce an instrument called an *atali*, that is, a small lever whose shape is this:
And let it have a short, somewhat thick end, and let it not be bent so that it does not break.
Then if by this the root is extracted, good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is a triangular end which has some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a more subtle end.
And you are helped with this instrument having two prongs, of which this is the form approximately the following: and with other instruments which precede in the extraction of teeth; for help is done with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, and this is the form:
of the triangular, curved or short end, which has some thickness, and is not bent so that it is not broken.
And you should know that the instruments for loose teeth are similarly made to the other instruments.
But the skilled and subtle artisan, finds for himself according to what the operation and the various illnesses signify to him; for there are illnesses to which the ancients did not give or devise a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken or injured, then it should be treated in its place with that which is convenient for it from among the instruments and forceps which we have described in the extraction of tooth roots, and help yourself with a splint of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely set; and then restore the area with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, their shape is flawed, especially in women, and especially in venerable old women; therefore you must consider: if a tooth is adjacent to another tooth and closure or alignment is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you should incise it with an instrument of which this is the form; and it is similar to a pig's tusk, and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp point.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are damaged.
And if the protruding tooth is amenable to filing, then file it with an Indian iron file, of which this is the form:
And let the entire thing be of Indian iron and very carefully shaped; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some scrapers.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
On the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated it with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then to prevent them from falling out, devise a way to strengthen them with gold wire, and not silver, because silver turns green and is easily powdered after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of application is that you take the wire and pass double the length needed between two healthy teeth, and then so that it grows with two twists of the wire between the damaged teeth, whether one or more, until the wire reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving to the point where you began, and tighten it carefully and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you tighten the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends in each place, and cover them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightened like a Roman [brace/bandage/binding].
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straightening of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, thus as you see these bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to the proper alignment of each in their places, and are regrown as we have recounted, and that they remain fixed.
And this is only done by good craftsmen and wise [men/people].
And sometimes some material from cow bones is prepared, and it is made from them in the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is fastened as we have said, and it remains and serves in that way for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which occurs in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is found under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on him is that you open the mouth of the patient, his head being in your lap; and elevate his tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its restriction.
But if in that [area] there is some hardness or knot, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you should incise and divide that across its width until you have cut the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the front of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
Then let the site of the incision be washed with warm water or with wine vinegar or with cold water; and then place under the tongue linen cloths; that is, a piece of cloth which the patient should keep under his tongue every night so that it does not heal again [in the wrong way].
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat that [wound] with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Concerning the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess similar to a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting in front of the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined towards white, and there is moisture in it, then you place your thumb there, and incise it with a thin spatula, and incise it on all sides; and if blood flows during your operation, then place crushed verdigris (or vitriol) on it until the blood is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completed it, until it is washed with sour wine and salt; and treat it with the other remedies for this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the extraction of an abscess from the two ears and from that which is located in the throat and other abscesses.
When a goiter develops in the throat, these are glands that are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwards and are called “two ears”, and when you treat them with what we have said in the previous section and it is not healed, then you consider whether the abscess is hard, dark colored, and insensitive; then do not touch it with an iron instrument.
