And if it is red and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then you will either extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is one that should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already been reduced by complete rest or if it has diminished in any way; and then make the patient sit before the sun, and their head should be on your lap, and open their mouth, and the servant, taking hold with their hands, should press down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument, of which this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or brass, and let it be thin like a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with it and the abscess is shown to you, and you look at it carefully, then take an oncia and place it in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Also, if you pull out some matter from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Also, if you pull out some matter from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, gargle with a decoction of pomegranate bark or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two tonsils, and incise those as I have said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat, which abscess was dark in color, with little sensation, which had already closed her throat, and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and drink of water, and she was close to death if she had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had created two passages through which it exited through two openings.
Then I treated it fistularly and inserted an instrument into one of the openings, and I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn out, and immediately I incised it from where I understood the other opening was, and immediately I opened its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and immediately I inserted the instrument into that abscess.
And immediately I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then the woman's throat was completely free, and she immediately began to drink water, and soon after she ate food, then I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess succeeded it in that place, which I incised until it was completely resolved.
Then I took hold of it and cauterized her throat; then I stopped the addition [of medication or treatment]; and immediately I went for a walk and I didn't know what God would do about it then.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of a wen when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular [abscess]; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your treatment is not effective, and you see that the cauterized abscess is still there and is subtle, then you should incise that which is round and aggregated; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and it is not sensitive to the touch, then you should postpone its incision, for in that case there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner that I have described, in its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit and press their tongue with an instrument that follows its shape, and then place the hook on the uvula and pull it down and incise it with one of two instruments that are described in the incision of the two almonds [tonsils].
And you must not incise it except what is exactly at the natural limit without addition, because if you incise more, you will injure their speech.
And for the incision, use what we have recounted in the incision of the two almonds [tonsils], and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by its incision, you should use ingenuity in its cauterization without fear, and the manner of cauterization is only with a sharp water [likely referring to a caustic solution], and it is that you place the patient's head in your lap, and then press their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take of the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with that live coal [or heated instrument], and do not apply it thickly or thinly, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the form:
Let the extremity of it be a concavity in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that the saliva or mucus from their mouth flows out because of the internal medicine, so that nothing of it descends onto their throat and harms them.
Then take your hand with the medicine, and press it onto the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine, and introduce the spoon with the cotton into a tube above so that the cotton adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as is necessary to achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it, for it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it, and after the cauterization wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and clean with that what remains around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat externally with embrocation [a topical application], and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured by a method gentler than cautery or incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, dried Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and combine all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sharp wine and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with suitable clay, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole over which a concave brass instrument is placed,  in this manner; it may be made of silver or brass.
And insert the end into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the uvula through the small tube, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, for it would aggravate the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but rather cauterize the declared abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a cane and attach a hen's egg shell to the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is protected, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of thorns and that which adheres to the throat and of other causes.
Many times, a fish bone or some other thing adheres to the throat.
Then it is necessary that you extract what is visible of those things, upon which your sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be shown to you what is in the throat.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, tie it firmly with thread, and let the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the thorns are, it will extract them, and then they will be expelled. And moreover, take an eel, bind it with thread, and let it be inserted and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly pull it out.
But if they are not expelled by those means which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be somewhat thicker than a finger, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you may easily insert into his throat.
And let him have his head raised up, and be careful that you touch his throat bone, so that it may happen to him that with that you push the bone or thorn, or that the physician do so with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself insert it, because of his knowledge of the location of the lodged cause, and push downwards, and pull his hand up with the instrument, all this according to the amount that is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not help, then consider placing it in the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed their tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, careful movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this repeatedly (until the leech is dead and leaves the throat) and abstain from giving the patient any water that day.
Then take a vial full of cold water, and open their mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow anything from it and agitate the water every now and then with your hand, for the leech, as soon as it feels the water, falls off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cymene or asafoetida through the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of this is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is fitted into that hole; and then begin the fumigation; and have the patient put their mouth to the end of the cannula, and close their mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall off.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus necessarily it should be expelled with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps as you sometimes see, except that it has some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughening of a file, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the opening of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many kinds, in many ways, according to the remembrance of them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses in themselves; and by that which we understand of the humors.
And the second way is according to the places in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, some abscesses are such that they should not be perforated except near the maturation of the pus in them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw and not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until it may consume that which is around it, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that limb is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured it will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it unripe; and it is not fitting to perforate it raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that its matter may not spread and be elongated to the parts within the anus, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it is not healed. Moreover, you must know the time of the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and also the diminution of the hardness and of the pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know the perforation should fall in the place lowest in the abscess and furthest from the healing eminence.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and away from the tendons, the cords of the nerves, the arteries; and, finally, in all equal places in which there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you delay its perforation until its digestion is confirmed according to what has been said; for if you perforate it before that, the pus is prolonged and much filth is formed, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body because of necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation or many incisions according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And there are other abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be incised and curved, when it is made like bread and is already ripe, as happens in cases of carbuncles and buboes.
And some of these are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And some of them are treated with a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar to those.
But in those that are not like the superficial boils, you should perforate it with only a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and do not attempt to extract all the pus at once; having extracted some of it, then dress the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger portion, and do this many times gradually until it is all removed.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in a decrepit state, because the vital spirit is often dissolved with the egress of the pus, and the patient dies very easily, and you will open them.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you must cleanse the wounds and consider then if the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then you should apply lint or old linen to it; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you should place a wick in each opening until they come together.
And if a part of the skin is cut away in the abscess, or you have hollowed it out around the edges, then you should fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have extracted splinters or roots, without moisture; and dress it on the third day; and then proceed and treat it with the ointments that are needed until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you dip a linen cloth, and apply this to the bleeding area many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer the treatment and care and powders which I have mentioned in many places in this our book and division.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should infuse powders in hot wine and oil, and apply them to the area.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its treatment what is appropriate until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally; but according to the way of distinction, I have already spoken of each abscess and in what manner is the way of its treatment, in separate chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses occur, and they are a kind of nodes which contain membranes that are attached to them; and their kinds are many.
And of them, some are purulent, and those contain a humidity similar to lime (or possibly referring to a viscous, lime-like consistency). And of them, some contain a humidity resembling dust, and similar to them; and of them, some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of them, there is no fear, since the extraction of them does not cause contrary [harm/injury/a reaction].
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is like this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if that which it contains is humidity, then incise it with a cut along its length, with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G. Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you should scrape that skin that contained the humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, for often it returns if any of it remains.
And then submerge cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode everything that remains of that membrane.
And then repeat on that cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remnant of that membrane; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you should incise it according to this form: with an incision having the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from all parts, and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if your incision meets with resistance, then do what I recounted to you first.
And similarly proceed in a petrified abscess, in incision and cure, as we have told you; and the incision on a petrified abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little humidity.
And sometimes some of these abscesses, which are in the head in some men, contain no humidity at all; the reason is that I incised an abscess in the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head besides this one, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that occur on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of these should all be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those from the lowest part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for that cure.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42: On the incision of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses happen in the neck, and in the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those which occur in the neck, many are generated among themselves, and every scrofula of those is within its own tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for among them are some stony, and some that contain humidity, and some are malignant which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that which you see to be of good disposition on touching, whose manifest color is close to the color of the flesh, and is softened throughout, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, you should incise it with a superficial incision from the upper to the lower part of the body along the line of G within the line of B, and scrape it from all sides, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the instrument not be too sharp, lest you injure your hand with the incision, or disturb the patient, and lest you incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you should incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and compress the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to be purulent. When the blood is severed/cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with your index finger if there is any cause of other small scrofulae; then incise those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, it is necessary then that you not incise that scrofula at its root. Beforehand, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then care for it.
And if you incise the entire scrofula, then you must bring together the two lips of the wound and stitch it immediately so that no superfluity remains.
But if you see that superfluity remains because of the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you understand how to incise the root of it, and remove it and administer suture and what is said [above/before].
And for those scrofulas that contain humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and position the perforation so that it is at the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound is already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the head of the abscess accidentally occurring in the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the head [of the abscess], and no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the written account of those men's words.
But in men having a throat abscess, the extraction of the head [of the abscess] must be avoided, because no benefit is gained thereby, since all the veins and major arteries of the patient are located there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth, throat, or both ears, when the disease is not in the throat passage [esophagus/trachea], it is necessary to use the incision of the head [of the abscess] to avoid the harm that is caused by suppuration.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we must incise the head of the abscess below three circles of the circles of the abscess, or four with a small incision in the width between two circles in such a quantity that the incision is in the fatty tissue, not in the cartilage; and this location is suitable for incision, because it is free of flesh and its blood vessels are far away.
And if the physician is timid, then he should extend the skin of the throat with forceps, and then carefully incise the skin until he reaches the abscess, but avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
And then incise the fatty tissue as we have recommended; and the incision of the throat is indicated by the inflammation that has come out of it with what is broken from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the time has arrived in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the skin of the wound, and sew it up alone, without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the discourse which we have recounted, is an exposition that shows that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some proceed to incise the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe through the area of the wound for some respiration, and be saved from death.
And for that reason, I command that the wound be left open until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I command that the wound be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this I have seen with my own eyes: a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and cut with it a part of the windpipe; and I was called to her treatment, and I found the wound as it is found in those bitten, and I uncovered the wound and found blood which had flowed from the small wound.
Therefore, I was convinced that she had not cut a vein or artery, and air was issuing from the wound, and I treated it until it was healed.
And there happened to the servant nothing except hoarseness in her voice, and after some days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the section of the epilepsy there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Chapter 44. Of an incision concerning an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess called throat hernia is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and it is very common in women.
And it is according to two manners, for it is either natural or accidental.
And in the natural [type], there is no way to cure it.
And the accidental is according to two manners, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other manner resembles an abscess which is made by the knotting of an artery, and in that there is danger. And it is not fitting to act presumptuously with iron, except in that which is small. And you probe that and investigate that with the finger, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you incise it as with nodes, and extract it with that which contains it from the skin and a blister; and if not, then its whole being is examined, and then cure the place with that which is necessary for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Of the incision of the kinds of nodes.
There are many kinds of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their kinds in the division.
And it is necessary that I recount thus the difference between a node and a protrusion, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that with a protrusion there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is sedated; and putrefaction is complete, whereupon the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that lymphatic humor which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a bladder, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two kinds, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division.
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, that you expel it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called a probe, according to how its form will appear in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will rotate your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the membrane.
And then place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then remove the probe and consider what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing humidity, whatever its color, is expelled, then you incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if no humidity comes from the track of the probe, then know that it is a fatty tumor (or lipoma).
And dry it out with an incision in the form of a cross, according to this form: as I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and peel off the skin from all sides easily, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract the pus with the node.
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the node will return again.
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound, and place on it a hot poultice, and treat it with another cure until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you sew the two lips of it and treat it with that which causes it to grow flesh.
But if a pulsating vein is opposite it, or a non-pulsating one, then you consider and fill the place with crushed vitriol and leave it tightly bound for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood ceases; and then return to incising that which remains of the node.

Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to the habit of sleeping, and then the breasts become inflamed, and they resemble the breasts of women, and remain apostematous, swollen.
And to one who has this, it is necessary that he incise those with a lunar section, according to this form, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then join the two lips of the wound with suture, and care for it until it is healed.
But if the breast droops downwards and is softened by its size, as happens in women, then let it be split in two, in its upper parts, with two incisions similar to a lunar figure, each contained within the other, the end of one within the other, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this form, from line B and line G. And then scrape the skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said concerning the suture and the powders, that which is necessary in the cure until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what must be incised because of the patient's unease and the flow of blood, then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the perforation of abscesses that form under the armpit.
These abscesses that form under the armpit are a kind of scrofula, hard, containing a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity must be incised with a lunate incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old linen and leave it there until the next day.
And then the linen should be removed and treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the kind of nodes and scrofulas, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofulas.
But if the wound is so extensive that it produces putrefaction, then administer cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from archery or bloodletting.
When an artery is broken and the clot that forms on it is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or vein: for when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long and attached deeply in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a kind of grating or creaking sound within it.
And that which is due to a vein is round and superficial on the body.
And incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially in that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and if it is very large, then its treatment with iron (surgery) should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should also be avoided [i.e. incision should be avoided].
But concerning that which is there because of inflammation of the opening of the artery, make a longitudinal incision in the clot; and then widen the incision with forceps, and then approach the artery, and free it from the surrounding tissues until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with a double ligature in two places —  a procedure which I have already shown you in the extraction of two arteries that were in the temples.
And then, press the area between the two ligatures with a spatula until all the blood that is there comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
And then administer treatment to the area where pus is generated until the ligatures fall off; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this purpose until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess caused by a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much as possible of the abscess with the clot, and then insert the needle below the area which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligature, as I have shown you in the ligature of a grape, in two parts, with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will pull out, then insert the thread with that needle under the entire abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the clot, and leave what is tied in it.
And then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens because of a twisted nerve.
Just as abscesses happen in arteries and veins, similarly abscesses happen in the nerves when they are hit or injured by excessive work, and similarly.
And it is mostly found in the places of the joints or the heel, or in any place where the joints are weakened.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body parts, and it is in the greatest part of its disposition without them, and when it is squeezed with force, the patient feels a similar numbness.
And the abscess is not aggregated in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the ribs, and it is weakened in every part and does not go to the front or back parts.
Therefore, for those [abscesses] that are in the joints, it is not suitable to be invaded with iron, because a contraction will quickly happen.
But that which is in the head or on the forehead, treat it with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and excise it from the roots.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs from that which happens to the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies,  tumors which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and the lips of these are turned inward, and some are small, and some are very large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two tumors which were very similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with thin roots.
And the lips of these were turned inward and split open, and humidity constantly flowed from them.
Therefore I incised them and found in one about eighteen ounces, and in the other about six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the tumor is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let cauteries be in your presence in the fire, because many times after its section much blood is expelled; and if the blood overwhelms you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind it to the tumor, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease tightening the lead greatly over the tumor, until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the tumor has a deep root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the abdomen.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then treat it until the wound is healed.
And be careful that you do not presume to incise a tumor which is dark-colored, insensitive, and of slow growth, for that is likely cancerous.
And I come to the remembrance of cancer concerning this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of umbilical hernia.
Umbilical hernia is caused by many things; either by fissures of the umbilicus which is on the abdomen, and from it the membrane or intestine protrudes as happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or by constricted flatulence within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the umbilicus and protrusion of the membrane, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its location is as we have recounted, with even greater variability, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and quickly returns; and it is very easily felt there, and is greatly increased by the entrance of a bath and strenuous work.
But if it is due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the swelling is hard, large and firm, and is located in a specific place.
But if it is due to wind/gas, then touching it feels soft.
And the operation on it is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is from an artery, or from a vein, or from wind/gas; then you should be forbidden from treating it, because in that there is fear and danger, just as I taught you in the chapter in which I spoke of swellings from an artery and from a vein.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is from the intestine or from the membrane, then you should order the patient to hold his breath, and stand extended; and then mark with a probe around the entire umbilicus, and then order him to lean between your hands on his belly, and then surround with a broad spatula the circumference of the umbilicus over the area which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling upwards with a large finger; and then bind around with strong thread or with a bandage, and tie it in a suitable manner.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and seek the intestine; and if you have found it and held it by the ligature, then you carefully extract and push the intestine into the interior parts of the belly.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that with a finger; and incise its superfluities.
But if it is an artery or vein contrary to you, then surround that area, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put on them two strong threads and insert the two needles into the circumference which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh dries and falls off by itself; or incise it when it is thinned and dried, and then treat it with whatever ointments are necessary until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is of raw flesh or from humidity, then it is necessary that you concavate the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find there, and then treat it with what is necessary and which consolidates the area.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken, in the division, of the types of cancer and in what manner it is possible to treat it with medicines, and that it is better to avoid treating it with iron so that it is not ulcerated; and we have described cancer engendered in the womb and caution regarding its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similar members, to extract it all, and especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, one should not meddle with it, for I myself could not heal anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone else who has succeeded.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and leave the patient and purge the black bile sometimes, and then bleed the patient if a manifest repletion is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which the location of the operation is included, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape it completely with a good, sharp curette until no part of its roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not staunch it lightly, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood completely with your hand or with whatever instrument is available to you among your instruments.
But if you find excessive thick blood from a vein incision or an artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood ceases, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy from water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or the species of anasarca, because that is mortal.
And when you have treated this species of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is ineffective, then consider: for if the patient has already acquired weakness, or has another illness along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain, and similar things, then be careful not to treat him with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with him there is no other illness but dropsy alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the manner of his operation is that you raise the patient between your hands, and the attendant then presses his belly with his hand, driving the water towards the lower part of the abdomen, and then take a sharp-pointed instrument in this manner:
Let it be two-sided, sharp and long, with a pointed end like a spatula; however, let there be some slight shortness in it, so that in the operation with it you do not penetrate the intestine and injure it.
And then consider if the generation of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you, with the incision, distance yourself from the navel by the amount of three fingers towards the lower parts, directly over the pubis.
But if the generation of the dropsy is due to a liver ailment, then your incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that fragile place.
Then perforate the entire peritoneum with the instrument, and then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the omentum, and you shall scrape that; and if it is the quantity of a fingernail or thereabouts.
Then perforate the omentum until the spatula reaches the empty space, which is the location of the water. Then extract that and introduce an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tinned lead, which at its lowest part has a small hole, and on its sides it has three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, thus:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, for when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately rests upon the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate amount, for if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient will quickly die from the dissolution of his animal spirit, or a syncope will occur, which is near to death.
But evacuate it according to the patient's strength and according to what the patient's condition, pulse strength, and overall well-being indicate to you.
Then remove the instrument, and retain the water which is the cause, for it is immediately retained by the chain which covers the hole which is above the cup; concerning which I have recounted to you that you should perforate it in that manner.
And then repeat the procedure the next day if you see the patient tolerating it, and remove a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and be careful not to remove more than a little water.
But if you fear for the patient and remove a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and abundant sweating in a bath, and exposure to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 55.
On the treatment of children born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born whose urinary meatus is not perforated (or the opening is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, using a very fine probe, according to this method:
And then place a thin lead pin in the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated, and it will urinate; and then return the pin.
And if you do not place the lead [pin], it is possible that the urine, which goes to that place, will not allow it to close.
But that one whose opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for many days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its place, so it is said, the cause of which is that in those, the one that is born and the opening is below the end of the penis, so that it cannot urinate towards the inner parts, until it elevates the penis with its hand towards the upper parts; and it is not generative, because the semen cannot reach the womb correctly, and it is a very long-lasting illness.
And the manner of the operation on him is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with vigorous traction, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the prepuce with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or that you shave some part, so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis and that the opening falls in the middle place as it should.
And beware of the flow of blood in your operation, for that often happens; and stop it with something that absorbs the blood, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Of the whitening which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
This whitening often occurs on the penis and is a swelling of fleshy matter.
And of that, one is malignant and the other is not malignant.
If it is not malignant, you should suspend it with a fine hook and excise it until you have removed it all; then apply to the wound an ointment immersed in Egyptian ointment; and afterwards, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the whitening is malignant, of a leaden color, then you should apply cautery near its incision and its root.
But if the whitening is on the foreskin of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the whitening is inside the foreskin and the other part is outside, then you should remove the whitening which is inside first, until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside, because when you treat both at the same time, it is not certain that it will not be damaged.
And if you find in the genitals and foreskin blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and is about to corrupt or is already corrupted; and then apply to that a poultice of honey, mixed with crushed and sifted flour from pomegranate rind and rye.
And then treat it with another cure until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
Because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is, for bleeding and for the wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you must insert into the opening of the foreskin a lead cannula so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis (and this adhesion occurs only in one whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision has not been performed; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the adhesion is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these exquisitely, then it is necessary that you scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and of the foreskin, and this is because the foreskin, when it is thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the foreskin from the head of the penis with a very thin linen cloth, which has already been wetted in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is healed.
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is done primarily on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest method leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not describe circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience, which is why I have found the majority of surgeons and barbers using circumcision and cleansing with a scalpel and with towels; and they use ligation with thread and incision with the nail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with scissors, with ligation with thread than cleansing with the nail, because many times the skin peels off from above, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which cobblers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not ensured, because it easily enters the hole of it.
But in the operation with a nail, you easily make a mistake and corrupt your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with scissors and ligatures with thread, the goodness, the experiment reveals to me, because the scissors are of proportional incision, for that reason the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below; and therefore as you put your hand, there are two proportional sharpnesses, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and a compression of the thread is made with a figure containing the foreskin of the glans from every part, with which no error at all occurs.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child stay, especially if it is one of those who understands a little, that you do nothing but tie the thread to its glans only, and leave it until the next day.
And then do that in such a way as is possible for you, considering his understanding. And then make the child lie still between your hands, prepared and not fidgeting; and hide the scissors in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them nor any other of the instruments.
And then put your hand to his glans and blow on the foreskin, and pull it until the head of the glans is exposed, and then cleanse it of whatever filth is attached to it.
The shape of the scissors for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be blunt and the other sharp, so that the nail head does not protrude, and let the length of the two points be equal to the length of two fingers, more or less.
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the whole inner foreskin, or part of it, from below the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extend that [foreskin] with your nail before the suppuration of the place and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your nail, then pull it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed penis, then you leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and carefully remove the skin, and incise it as you have prepared.
And guard the head of the penis, for that will not harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure from the scrotum, and it obeys the parts above, that will not harm him.
And moreover, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh, and similar things to those we have mentioned in the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said, and the cause is vehement in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and be a thin, hollow cone, like a bird's quill, with the subtlety of a long ray, in the similitude of a palm and a half long, to which there should be a thin tube at its tip.
And the manner of drawing out the urine with this is that you take a doubled bladder and tie wool or cotton to its end with a good binding; and introduce the ends of the bladder into the lower part of the syringe, and cut with scissors if there is any superfluous wool, but introduce it into the cannula with force. And then lubricate the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and rub their bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rosewater.
And then introduce the syringe easily until it reaches the root of the penis; and then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it has reached the empty area, and it is not done according to this order, but the meatus, through which it goes, is tortuous.
And then extend the bladder with the wool little by little, because the urine will wet the wool.
Then extract it and make a cure for the urine, and use a syringe; do not cease to do this and use the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a catheter and with similar instruments which are suitable for this.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or congealed blood in it, or hardened pus, and you want to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that at its extremity it is blunt, that is, closed. And in it there are three holes: two on one side, thus at times.
In the beginning, it is a concave place, in which that which is expelled is held, and it should be according to the quantity that fills it, without addition, until when some cause is drawn from the humors with it, it is drawn, and when it is expelled, with that it is drawn, and when it is expelled long, as the catheter does with which naphtha is inserted in sea battles.
Therefore, when you want to draw humors from the bladder, insert the end of the catheter into the humor, and draw with it that which is pressed towards the upper parts, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the urethra as we have recounted in the syringe; and then expel the humor with that which you expel, because that humor immediately reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it.
The form of the subtle catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the upper end should resemble a small feather, and below it a slender tube.
And then take a goat's bladder, and place in it the liquid you want to instill into the bladder, and then tie it tightly between two [things] with a strong double thread, and heat that liquid over the fire.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the penis.
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has already reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it throughout its circumference; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is tightly bound; and then seal the circle in such a way that it is sealed like a [sealed] vessel, after you put into it what you want of liquids, oils, and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with that as you have done with the bladder, and express with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already mentioned in the division the types of stone and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and a stone generated in the bladder, and their signs.
And we have mentioned the stone in which [one performs] an operation with the hand, and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that the stone generated in the bladder mostly affects children; and among its signs is that the urine that is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his groin, and often cries out in pain, and is restless; and with this, the anus protrudes in many of those [affected].
And the healing of children from this is easy until they reach fourteen years; and it is difficult to cure in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those [extremes - children and the elderly].
And of that [stone] which is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is contrary to that [i.e., more difficult].
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we cleanse the patient with a clyster, which will remove all the phlegm that is in his bowels; because this phlegm sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone through its examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up and be massaged and manipulated downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or that it sometimes jumps from another location.
Then make that [patient's body] lie between your hands prepared, and his hands should be under his thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to incline downwards.
And then search for it [the stone] and all of it [the stone and its surroundings] thoroughly.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then immediately prepare yourself for incision upon him.
And if it does not entirely descend upon your touching, then it is necessary that you anoint with oil your index finger of your left hand, if the patient is a child, or your middle finger if he is a fully grown young man, and introduce it into his anus. And search for the stone until it is under your finger. And bring it back little by little into the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards towards the place where you will make the incision.
And command the assistant to press the bladder with her hand; and command another assistant that with her right hand she extend the genitals upwards, and with her other hand she hold back the perineum which is below the genitals, in the area where the incision is to be made.
And then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and cut in that area which is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the external incision is wide and the internal incision narrow, according to the size that the expulsion of the stone allows, and no larger, for you will easily compress with your finger, which is in the anus, the stone during the incision, and it will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and rough, which makes their expulsion difficult.
And of those, that which resembles an acorn, and is round, is easily expelled.
Therefore, if it is one with angles and roughness, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not expelled in this way, then you should push on it, or press on it with a suitable instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and do not let it escape; or introduce under it a slender instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot push on it, then slightly enlarge the opening; and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if there is more than one stone, then initially place your hand on the opening of the bladder, and then cut on it; and then push it gently towards that; and do similarly if there are many more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is folly to cut it with a large incision, for this causes one of two things to happen to the patient: either death or continuous urinary incontinence, because the area is not entirely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion until it is expelled, or work on its fracture with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood, and bind it tightly. And place over that a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient on their back, and do not unbind the bandage until the third day.
And when you have unbound it, bathe the area with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there occurs a hot, burnt, and corrosive abscess, and of such appearance as if the blood were frozen in the bladder, and because of it the urine is obstructed (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it provokes corruption of the bladder and putrefaction thereof.
Then wash the wound with sharp wine, water, and salt, and with a poultice of whatever is suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during all the treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and thus the urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I have previously recommended before resorting to incision, for many times through this treatment it has been necessary to resort to incision; and I have proven this, that is, you take a heated iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular extremity, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take bile, and bind the penis below the stone with it so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches the stone. And rotate the perforator with your hand against that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, because that is broken, and it will be expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you for some reason that prevents it, then tie one bandage below the stone, and another bandage above the stone. Then incise upon the stone in that penis between the two bandages. And then extract it. And then untie the bandages and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bandage below the stone except so that it does not return to the bladder, and the other bandage above, because when the bandage is untied near the extraction of the stone, the stone returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this it is necessary for you, when you tie the bandage above, that you lift the stone to the upper parts or that it return during your procedure, and thus close the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones in women are rarely generated.
But if a stone is found in one of them, then its cure is difficult, and is forbidden by many methods.  (The last part implies that many methods of treatment are contraindicated, because of the difficulty, or that the condition is considered incurable by many.)
One reason is that women easily become virgins again (i.e., their virginity is easily restored; or perhaps referring to a perceived fragility that makes treatment risky).
And the second is that you will not find a chaste woman, or a married woman, who willingly reveals herself to the physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the manual operation.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for stones in women is far from the stone itself, wherefore a deep incision is needed, and in this there is danger.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must find a female physician who is knowledgeable, and they are rarely found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a skillful and subtle physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who is somewhat skilled in this art.
Then have that woman present, and command her to do everything you instruct, from the initial examination for the stone; the reason is that if she determines the woman is a virgin, then she must insert her finger into her rectum and search for the stone.
Then, if she finds it, grasp the stone with her finger, and then instruct her to make an incision over it.
But if she is not a virgin, then instruct the matron to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with good pressure.
Then, if she finds it, she must carefully manipulate it from the opening of the bladder towards the lower parts, using the sum of her skill until she reaches the root of the matter.
And then incise over it, near the middle of the vagina, near the root of the matter wherever it is stopped, and center the stone in that area, and her finger must not be removed from the stone while holding it below.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert a probe into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size which you know the stone will be removed from it.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore you should know how many types there are of it so that you may signify that which you wish.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and keep it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And see that you prepare with you the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you may be aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the place and bind it tightly with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will very quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the place has healed, then you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white sheath which is below the scrotum, containing the testicle, and is called the sheath.
And sometimes it is in the proper tunic which nature has prepared for the testicle, so that it appears that there is another testicle; and this is the scrotum between the white sheath which we have mentioned, and this is only fat.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it occurs due to a blow to the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black and foul-smelling, or a white watery color, and this is the most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sheath which we have mentioned, then the abscess is round, leaning slightly towards length like the shape of an egg, and it surrounds the testicle, because the moisture contains it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper tunic, then the abscess is round near the testicle and therefore one believes it to be another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the covering of the genitals and the white sheath, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.
And if you want knowledge of the color of the moisture, then you test the color of the moisture in the abscess with a probe, the form of which precedes; and according to what comes out on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its cure with iron, then it is necessary that we order the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and see his body full.
Then let him be supine on his back on something a little high, and place many cloths underneath him.
Then you sit on his left side, and order the assistant to follow on his right side, who will extend his rod to one of the two sides of the covering of the genitals and to the part of the navel of the abdomen.
Then take a broad spatula and split the covering of the genitals from the middle along its length almost to the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made straight and equally distant from the line that separates the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the white fat included.
And scrape that, and observe, and be careful not to cut into it; and your scraping with which you scrape it, let it be done so that it is suspended with the eggs, and most subtly scrutinize the scraping as much as possible for you.  (This sentence is difficult to translate perfectly due to the archaic medical terms and ambiguous phrasing.  "Suspended with the eggs" likely refers to a delicate, careful approach, possibly relating to anatomical structures near the testicles).
Then perforate the water-filled fat with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the fat to the upper parts; and do not touch the covering of the genitals within, and incise the fat in whatever way is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not very carefully scrutinize its incision, there is no assurance that the water will not return.
But if the egg protrudes from its covering during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the fat, return it inside, and then close the two lips of the covering of the genitals with sutures, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.  ("Egg" here likely refers to a testicle or related structure.)
And if you find the egg corrupted by another illness, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the suspensory ligament, and extract the egg, and then treat it as we have said.  (Again, "egg" likely refers to a testicle.)
But if the fluid is collected in two separate parts, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that part in the same way that you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is a single operation, then you perform it.
And then you shall wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the miracle of the belly; and place over it doubled cloths and powders, and bind them over with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then let two cloths be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and let the ends be made with which it is bound of cotton or damp wool, and let them be attached with a bandage in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends shall reach from the buttocks to the groin, and the second shall reach to the part of the scrotum, and two ends over the two thighs, and two other ends over the two knees, and the whole bandage shall be adjusted in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white cyphus containing the water is uncovered, and then you take a cautery according to this form: and it resembles the Greek letter.
And then perforate the cyphus with it, and ignite it until all the humidity is expelled.
And then stretch the cyphus with forceps, and scarify it with forceps, and scarify it with a thin ignited cautery; and incise it as much as possible until you eradicate all of it; and beware of the egg that the fire does not touch it.
And know that this operation is safer and causes less blood flow, and is better than an operation with incision.
But if an abscess forms contrary to your expectations during the incision or cauterization, or there is excessive bleeding, then you must cure whatever opposes you in this, with a treatment that is appropriate to it as described.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I recommend it; and it is when you are certain, through what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some elevated seat, then perforate the abscess at its lowest part with a broad spatula, and place the perforation according to the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough that all the water drains out.
And then place a cotton cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water may drain. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the drainage of the water, at that time you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white panniculus at the opening of the wound causes resistance and prevents the drainage of the water; then you should insert into the wound a probe or instrument with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which has been described previously; or adjust a small [opening] in the obstruction of the wound.
And know that the water will return, and it will accumulate again within about six months or thereabouts; and when it has accumulated, then let it be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus spend his days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a danger leading to death in most cases; therefore, in my opinion, its removal and being saved by that [method] is better.
And I am recalling the operation on that [hernia] and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the bodies of which the genitals are composed, and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity, which is poured into the genitals, or by a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a lapified abscess, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by inflammation of these, so that its appearance is perceived, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it is not advisable that one touch it.
