But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means through compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision is made on this area and on the other tissues; and it is so that you incise the tunic of the genitals, and then extend the testicle to the upper parts, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament of the vessels, and ligate them, and incise the suspensory ligament after you free it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then you must extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then you must free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh at the site of the adhesion which is behind, then you must incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia that is of the scrotum.
A scrotal hernia is an abscess enveloped by some covering similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, it is necessary to consider what preceded it.
However, we must describe the operation in the same way that the first physicians did.
And I say that you must have the patient sit on a raised seat; then gently push the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers, along with the vessels that are felt near the penis; and have another assistant hold and stretch those parts forcefully.
And then incise with a sharp, broad scalpel, making a transverse incision directly over the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
And then scrape the entire area as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads which are lodged in the temples.
And then insert a double-threaded needle into those [vessels], and tie it first at the place where you cut the veins, and tie it again at the other end. And then incise those [vessels] in the middle lengthwise with a straight incision, and extract whatever is accumulated of foul and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds where you want healing to be generated, so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitalia may fall [back into place], for this is not harmful.
But if you cut all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be tied off with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they wither, or that they be tied off so that there is no benefit from them.
Chapter 65.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen, on the side of the inguinal rings of the abdominal wall, causing the intestines to descend through that opening.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur from many causes: a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, or similar.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and there is an inguinal abscess near the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that it initially causes severe pain suddenly; and there is a different abscess appearing under the skin near the groin, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body is outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" is likely a variant or corruption of a term related to the omentum or its appearance).
And sometimes it is formed with flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and is retained there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of the types of this illness, iron is feared, therefore it is necessary to avoid cauterization in this case.
And the method of operating on this is that you command the patient to reduce the intestine with his hand to the inside of its cavity if it obeys its return, and then he should be placed on his side between your hands, and raise his legs.
And then hold the spermatic cord which is the cord of the testicles upwards, and incise the spermatic cord entirely lengthwise; and then insert into the two lips of the incision hooks, as I instructed, to open the incision with them.
And if the incision allows, as much as is possible for you, that the egg [testicle] may be expelled from it.
And then scrape away the coverings which are under the spermatic cord, until the hard white tunic of each part is uncovered.
Then you introduce your finger into that which is the testicle, into that which is between the white tunic, which is under the cord of the testicle, and between the second tunic, and separate with it the adhesions which are in the part behind the testicle.
And then gently manipulate the spermatic cord upwards with your hand, and with this extend the white tunic upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic towards the incision; and command the assistant to extend the testicle upwards, and you dissolve the adhesions which are behind with complete resolution.
And search with your fingers that there is no intestinal matter enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such matter, then you push it back down into the abdomen.
And then incise in the area below the spermatic cord, through an incision from which blood and pus will flow as we have recounted in what we have said.
And then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we mentioned near its incision to prevent bleeding, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed in warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Of a windy hernia (or,  Of a ventosity hernia).  The term "ventosa" here likely refers to a hernia accompanied by gas or air.
In this type of hernia, I have not seen anyone who has been healed by using iron (i.e., cautery or surgery with iron instruments).
And the first [physicians] said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with [or accompanied by] pus; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that [hernia] with ease; and then dry the middle area around the abscess with that which opens it, until the vessels fall (or collapse).
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that is made in the inguinal region (groin).
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And it does not allow the genitals any cause of the intestines in the genitals; and if it does, it is little; and it returns in all hours (meaning it keeps returning).
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fissure is formed in the inguinal canal until the intestine descends into the inguinal canal; and this happens because of the stretching of the inguinal canal, which is in the groin as we have said, which causes the inguinal canal to be stretched, and then it is softened and the area becomes inflamed and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its narrative has preceded. (Meaning:  The cure is cauterization, as previously described.)
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and it is that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the area of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with an incision in width according to the amount of three fingers.
And then tighten the white inguinal canal that is under the scrotum until it is uncovered.
And then take the ray (presumably a surgical instrument) and place it on the prominent part of the inguinal canal, and press it against the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent places on the end of the inguinal canal's ray (instrument), and join one to the other with suture.
And then remove the end of the ray (instrument), and do not completely incise the inguinal canal, nor extend the wound, nor anything else like what I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment of wounds, and as you incise the tissues, clean it and restore the wound until it is consolidated; because the inguinal canal is contracted and the prominence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this place, because it is closer to health (i.e., a more effective treatment).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the scrotum.
Many times the scrotum in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is spoiled.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie down according to his condition, and to sew up all the scrotum which is softened onto the natural scrotum; and then to join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew up the excess of the softened scrotum first with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then make incisions between the sutures; and then care for it with the treatment for other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
On castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but it is permitted for me to mention it in this book for a reason; and its mention is necessary only in two ways, one of which is so that it is within the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, so that he knows how to treat someone who has undergone it; and the other way is that we often have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit, such as goats, rams, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the method of operation is that we make the animal sweat in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then take them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear under the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and grasp the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise each testicle with an incision until both testicles protrude.
And then incise those after you have skinned each one. And do not place any substance on those of the cyphus (likely referring to a type of surgical instrument or wound), except for the fine cyphus which is on the vessels.
And this method of castration is better, which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves little substance of the testicles behind, so that the animal desires coitus less.
And then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is where it appears that there is a space in the place of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that space which is in the place of the testicles.
But in women, there is one type: and it is that the woman's vulva above the mons pubis is like two men's testicles, somewhat diminished in the outer part, like the foreskin of a man and two testicles.
And the cure is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those tightened tissues until the constriction is released, and then that you treat those with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the area of the testicles, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is often caused by natural reasons until it is pressed and examined; and sometimes it is enlarged in some women to such an extent that it is spread like in men and extends to the penis.
Then it is necessary that you hold the excess swelling with your hand or with a bandage and incision.
And do not overdo the incision, especially near the root, so that bleeding does not occur.
And then treat it with wound treatments until it is healed.
Moreover, a fleshy growth is flesh that grows in the mother's womb until it fills it, and it quickly protrudes outwardly resembling a tail (and for this reason some first called this "tailed disease"); therefore, it is necessary that we incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of arthritis.
Alratica is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by improperly formed flesh or by a subtle or thick septum.
And it is formed either in the anterior part of the vagina, or in its sides, or in its upper part, or in its lower part, and it prohibits coitus, and conception, and childbirth, and very quickly it prohibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examining the uterus; if the obstruction is apparent, that is sufficient; but if it is not apparent, then you must search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin septum, and it is close to the two labia, then you cut and break it up.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place the index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then forcefully spread the two labia until that thin septum is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and keep it moist all day so that the incised area does not close up.
But if the septum is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to flesh, then lift it with a hook, and incise it.
And have with you the medicines that stop bleeding without biting, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and enses, mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close up too quickly.
And keep it there for a few days and administer a poultice of dry herbs.
And then it should be treated with the other consolidation cure until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the mother's birth of other flesh [likely referring to a difficult childbirth or postpartum complication], and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a cranial abscess, the cranial abscess which is in the mother should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and of a boil or red swelling that occurs in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the orifices of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when they are old hemorrhoids, they become boils.
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the womb and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron (i.e., cautery or surgery).
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which one feels them, are those which are cured.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cold place and then extend the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them to the roots.
And then sprinkle on those boils the flow of blood with one of the incisive powders without biting, such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place her in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of  summach and myrtle bark and  woad and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to his back.
And if the blood is withdrawn, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, and to the nipples without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in comfrey juice or in ivy juice with red wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a wound by accident in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses happen in the mother,  such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcerous ones, and buboes, and herpes, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types and their signs, and their cures in the division (or section).
And it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which comes in the mother when it is among the abscesses which collect pus; and if you wish to know in what manner the operation is done with iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with throbbing and phlegm and fever, and sometimes redness and if the sensation comes upon it. Then do not attempt perforation, but cure it with what helps maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
And then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a stool that has two legs; and let her recline on her back and adjust her feet to the lower part of her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread, and place her arms under her knees, and tie each with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and administers the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will come in those things which will be done next.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you place with the ray the neck of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument is entered into the womb should be no more than the neck of the womb and harm the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the vertigine [likely referring to a rotating or twisting action or device] that runs in the instrument from the upper side, and that the attendant hold the instrument and rotate the vertigine until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and it is touched with the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that wound with a broad sponge.
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick submerged in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the calca [likely referring to a type of poultice or dressing] in the section in the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and over the vulva clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be treated for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lilies or of birthwort has been boiled, and then it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the enclosed womb, then you must be prohibited from treating it with iron, taking care with that which we have said in the section [presumably a previous section of the treatise].
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they emerge in an unnatural way/position.
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs, and it is that when you see the woman strain in the lower parts and desire to smell the air, and moreover is lightened by that which is above her of the birth, and hastens the emergence of the embryo, then you should know that this birth is according to natural form, and it is the emergence of the child head first, and the afterbirth with it or suspended or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily because when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and is cleansed of those superfluities with good purification/cleaning.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and undesirable, for when the embryo is emerged feet first and its body is emerged before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and quickly is turned upon its neck,  and similar to these undesirable forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and subtle in all these matters, and to guard herself from error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is involved in them so that it may be signified thereby and may be established thereupon.
When the fetus has emerged head first by natural effort, and this is arduous for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her vulva with cooked fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, spatula-shaped instrument between her labia and with it tears the secundine, or scrapes it with her fingernail until it is free of moisture. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be given a clyster of mallow and fenugreek mucilage with anise.
Then, after the clyster, instruct her to be compressed and make her sneeze with sneezing powder; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled first, then you must gently reduce them little by little. But if it does not obey the introduction, then place the woman on a stool and raise her feet, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remains of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it carefully, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, then you must push them upwards, and gently turn the fetus, and gently grasp it, and gently take one foot and extend it little by little.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled easily.
And if it is hindered by all that we have recounted, then take marshmallow mucilage and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, and gently apply this to the woman's vulva, at the very bottom of her belly. And gently make her sit in lukewarm water, until it reaches her hypochondria (the area below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts of her are softened, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and place it under her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and gently make her sneeze and hold her breath, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her belly with gentle pressure, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
Thus [deliver] the fetus laterally, holding [it with] one of your hands. Try to reduce [the dislocation] with your hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her hips with a vehement shaking, and then apply bandages which I have recommended made of mucilage.
And if childbirth has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction of the hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural shape, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus [deliver] the fetus over the neck, spreading your hands, and its face is towards its mother. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and that she does this easily.
And when it is grasped, then place on its head that which we have recommended of oil and mucilage.
And then instruct her to cough little by little, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and instruct her to lean on her right side, and then instruct her to strain; and make her sneeze with a potion. The prepared fetus exits on its side.
When the horror of the mother subsides according to this manner, consider, and then you incise the secundine with your nail until it is evacuated of its humidity.
And if the fetus is suspended over its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then gently push the fetus inwards until you place it as it should be, and tell the woman to strain several times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and seldom happens; but there are causes for this in nature, and they do not live.
And they share one placenta, even if they are multiples, and are separated by a septum, distinguishing them within the umbilical ligament of each. And sometimes they are expelled naturally and easily, as if it were a single fetus, and sometimes they are expelled unnaturally, as we have recounted.
And if any of them are difficult to deliver and it comes to what we have said about unnatural births, then you should treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and dexterity, and with patience, and is resourceful according to each case as she is prepared by her skill leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even more than ten are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which was determined by the measurement of glorious wise men.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife because it easily appears similar to her, and therefore she opposes it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Concerning the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication and it has not yet been expelled, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted with any of the accidents that make loss feared for her.
Then the woman should be placed on the bed on her back, with her head inclined downward and her hips raised.
And the attendant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
Then you moisten the mother's vulva with warm oils and with a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the location where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the areas near the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the heel or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that you hold the umbilical cord with your right hand, and place its curve in the space between the fingers of your left hand, and gently insert your hand and grasp the umbilical cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until you reach the empty amniotic sac.
Then add and place another umbilical cord section, or a third if necessary, alongside it, so that there is even traction, and not only extension along its straight length.
Rather, let it be pulled evenly from all sides, so that its extraction is eased, just as is done with a tooth before its extraction.
And it is necessary in this matter that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and expertly insert them and loosen what is retained with them.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then let the umbilical cords be moved to a higher position; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if one hand is delivered before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until it is completely delivered, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the wrist.
And thus you should do with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or a constriction occurs during its delivery, or there is accumulated water on its head, then it is necessary to insert between the fingers a spiny spatula and puncture the head and extract the water, or to open it with an instrument that is called an "alundat," the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is near this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with the alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is delivered but constricted below the clavicle, then it should be incised so that the fluid which is in the chest is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey delivery, then you incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it so that the fluid that is in it drains.
But if the fetus is positioned feet first, then its extraction is easier and its direction towards the mother's opening is easier.
But if it is constricted around the belly and chest, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the belly and chest so that what is inside them drains.
But if the other limbs are outside and the head remains inside, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's opening is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb, and with it searches for the head, and pulls it with her fingers towards the mother's opening, and then inserts into it one or two fingers, and pulls it out with them.
And if the mother's opening is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it must not be disturbed; instead, it is necessary to administer an infusion of moist, oily substances. And apply plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is lying on its side, then, if possible, it should be turned; and what we have said should be administered as for a living fetus.
And if that is not possible, then let it be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no part of the afterbirth inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of hot abscesses which may befall the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after, she became pregnant again, and soon after that, the fetus also died. A long time ago, an abscess occurred in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I drew out a bone, and after a few days, I drew out another bone; and I was very surprised, since there should be no bones in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in her menses, and she lived in this manner for a long time, a little pus being discharged from it.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is science and help that the skilled physician administers with his hand for a cure.
Of the forms of instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
The form of incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the lips are joined, to which joining there are two incisions at the two extremities of two poles/rods. However, this incision should be more subtle than the constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each rod be two fingers, and the thickness of each be one finger, and the length of each be a palm and a half; and in the middle space between the two rods there are two fastenings of the same wood, which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it is opened with them when you turn the two prescribed incisions.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, similar in appearance to forceps. However, at its extremity there are two additions like prongs, and the length of these additions is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with these, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, having opened what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with scissors, gradually, according to how much you want to open the mouth of the womb until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The form of the impellent by which the fetus is expelled:
The form of the hindrance by which the large head is obstructed.  (The "2" likely represents a superscript or annotation not relevant to the meaning).
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like tongs, and sometimes it is made long like forceps of this shape, like tongs, which have teeth like saw teeth, with which it is incised and extracted. Form of slight obstruction/hindrance:
The form of a hook having a spine/spike.
The form of a hook having two spines/spikes.
The form of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed/cut.
The form of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of those are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier in its operation and more abundant among men due to its quantity.
And do not neglect those instruments, whatever they may be, which are prepared by you, for the necessity of them is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and press your hand on her mouth and nose, and if by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid and place herbs in it, opening the mother's mouth;  such as rue, and mint, and chamomile, and Arabian scabious, and cassia lignea, and  holy thistle. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if after this treatment it remains and is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or marshmallow poultice, and then introduce it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when it is found, then take it and extract it little by little. But if it is attached to the uterus of the mother, then introduce her hand as we have recommended, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed so that the mother does not faint from the strong pulling. Afterwards, it should be turned to the right and left sides easily.
And if the mother's mouth (cervix) is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recommended, then take care not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction. However, what has emerged from her into the mother's mouth (cervix) should be left attached to the woman's genitals, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrapharmacum; for that will cause it to rot in a few days and it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it rots, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations appropriate for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients already approved of fumigation with hellebore and dried figs.
Method of instrumentation with which a woman is fumigated because of retained menses and secundines:
Let it be made of glass or brass.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation be placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold that until that fumigation is consumed and repeat with another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that stricture with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [likely referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below [presumably, in the anal region].
The fistulas that develop in the lowest part below are knots and swellings which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the parts.
And it is one fistula or several.
And when that swelling is longstanding and discharges a watery white fluid or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and by flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from discharge, and worms readily emerge from them.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the space, into the bladder, and into the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the hip joint and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the anus from one that is not penetrating, is that you insert your finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of lead or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything proceed from the opening, neither discharge, nor worms, nor flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then the sign of it is thus, urine from that [area], and prohibition of it [the area] so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then the sign of it is that the probe penetrates thus far if there is no tortuosity in it into the bone, and causes pain in the surrounding area; and that there is always pus from it; and that medication does not help it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no certain cure, and the treatment of those is the work and vanity of foolish physicians, on which they are fixated.
But in that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I shall recommend to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs towards you, his thighs being inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels it around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe itself penetrating on your finger, besides what you feel between your finger and that fistula or flesh, then you know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no cure in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it is healed in some men slowly/later.
And the medication by which help is hoped for him is that you ignite a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been explained before, and introduce that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fistulous, fleshy parts that resemble a bird's feather quill are cauterized.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen until all those cauterized tissues slough away, and then treat it with flesh-generating and consolidating medicines, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or fistula, and you see the fistula where it is the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see it drying with the probe, and let it be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the tip of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is released and removed.
And then cleanse those fistulous tissues which resemble a bird's feather quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood coming between you and your operation when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good things: that is, the cauterization of those affected tissues, and the stanching of blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flaxseed until the place becomes firm, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate hard flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and desiccation; when then it is restored, it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore with it.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a veil of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation there except through hope and trust (i.e., it's too risky to operate).
Which condition is treated in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that you can introduce the poultices and the treatment is close; and do not proceed much with incision lest you cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that such happen to the patient without their will; and then treat it with what we have said for it will heal quickly; or lengthen that veil and clean it  after drying it near the rectum, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp end, and then cleanse those fistulous areas which are in the incision where you can with escharotics; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the upper opening is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the part of the rectum, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the anus near the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing skins in this form:
and insert it into that [fistula] wrapped in five threads or around those; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates into the margin of the rectum inwards, then you extract the thread from that hole so that you can insert your finger into the rectum; and extract the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for one or two days, and when you cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well so that those areas of flesh are cut which are between the two ends of the thread and that they fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and consolidated.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, however it is prepared for you, except that it should be in the perineum, for it is not for you to do this so that you do not cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The form of a pointed probe with which the fistula is probed:  its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks similar to charcoal, or they are like grape seeds. And of these, some are small and some are large, and blood flows from them constantly, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, from which flows a citrine fluid or a little blood with continuous flow, and it is according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to strain and to express himself until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should grasp and lift them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks cannot grasp them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with rough cloth or cotton or a wad of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders at the incision, so that they act on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is previously described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey in its protrusion, then stimulate the patient with a mild laxative, and let it be washed with that which is in the anus and which will easily come out when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, it is necessary to suture it in this manner: take waxed thread and insert it into a needle and then draw the hemorrhoid to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle to the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and it is done laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then tie off the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them uncut, so that the excess blood may flow from that one.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointment or with another cure until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moistened and the crust is removed from them, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then remove them with more force than the first time until they bleed a lot and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that felon, that is, a whitlow, is nothing but a red swelling, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a clove, and it is found throughout the body and especially in the parts below the feet and fingers, and causes pain due to its swelling.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the whitlow be incised, and held with forceps or an instrument, and eradicated from its root; and then treat it.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was previously explained in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you stretch and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with thread or a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic).
But a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated, thick eminence on the surface of the body, growing often in the pubic area, and when it is rubbed, it is felt like the bite of an ant; and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture feather, and let it be strong, and place that over the mole so that the circle of the cannula contains it; and then move your hand with the cannula so that you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and place that in the depth of the flesh, and that you eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula, according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn to a point, blunt (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that the grip on it is eased and its rotation facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as previously described in the cauterization of athel in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments that are used to perform a christening (or possibly, a surgical incision/cauterization – the meaning depends on the broader context of the text).
Sometimes a christening (or incision/cauterization) instrument is made of silver, or lead, or cast or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made in small and large sizes according to those to whom it is administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are fragile or painful, the instruments for them should be small.
The form of the large and small christening (incision/cauterization) instrument:
The head of the cannula above, to which the pouch is attached, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a distinction where the pouch is attached, as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt (that is, not open) but rounded, tapering to a point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And the pouch, in which the medicines are placed, should be made of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its length should be one palm (handspan).
And it should be perforated with many holes, as it is coiled; and the space between the holes should be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw flax thread, about ten threads or so; and attach the pouch with it; and when the pouch is placed in position, tighten it at the head of the clyster with two distinct, strong ligatures, and then cauterize each hole with this.  The method of cauterizing with this is that you fill the pouch with medicine that is warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place a cushion underneath him, on which there is a mat or linen cloth; and then anoint the end of the clyster with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or something similar; and then insert it into the anus easily, and then the attendant should take the pouch and squeeze it with both hands and forcefully until everything in the clyster is evacuated into the bowels; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces a bowel movement, retain it as long as possible; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is even more beneficial.
But if you use this clyster for diseases of diarrhea and the bowel movement is frequent, then repeat the cauterization until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin and small, like those with which infants are given clysters.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was appropriate to the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with hand and with medicines according to the fulfillment/situation.  (The "fulfillment" here likely refers to the specific circumstances of the wound).
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what the wounds are made with and according to the place upon which the wounds fall.
And the causes for which wounds are often made are thus:  a stone throw, or an incision from a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar things of many causes.
But the wounds that are in the places of the body are thus: a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or feet, or belly, or thigh, and similar to those of the other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of certain wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or the like, and only the scalp is wounded and it is a large wound and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately bleed him and phlebotomize the cephalic vein without delay; and extract blood accordingly, according to the patient's strength, unless some other reason prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and coarse foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be reduced and he should not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a pledget soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if a hot abscess is certain, then place on the wound, if it is recent,  its own blood and the powder which I shall shortly recount, before the air has altered it.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from a sword cut or similar, and its lips are not approximated by themselves, then approximate them with sutures according to the manner of suturing I shall recount.
But if the wound has already exposed bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it but a slight suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the bone, and order the patient to be fed on foods which are rich in thickness: heads and things of similar consistency, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood may occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you should investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you to not do what we have said, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; thus you sprinkle this powder on them, for it consolidates them before the air alters them.
And the manner of making the powder is this: take oil of roses one part, dragon's blood two parts, burnt alum three parts; let all be very finely ground and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, unless it is abscessed, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes this alone suffices, when you do not have roses and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary to place it on them and they are fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially if it is in summer, then this powder is not suitable, but treat them with what we have said, so that you place on them some digestive ointments, or that you put on them powder from barley flour with water and honey until they stop festering; and then treat them with another cure until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the neck wounds.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple head wound.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are incised, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to do but to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as vitriol or similar substances, because they harm the nerves and contract them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as washing the wound with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and then, after washing with fresh water, applying other minor ointments in this manner, and moist unguents.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or approximate its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or pus collects in the lowest part, then perforate it at the lowest point.
But if an artery is incised in the wound and bleeding results, then separate or cut it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or any have been incised, and the veins of the neck are spared, then you should approximate the edges of the wound with sutures over the cervical spine and do not touch it; first align it and reduce it to its natural shape, and then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments which contain styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has damaged the bone, then clean it, and then if there are bone fragments, extract all those that you can; and what you cannot reach, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the injury is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle upon it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be recalled in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a venting of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you should place on it at the first hour a powder, and do not bind it tightly lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the patient; but rather place on it an attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not at hand, then you place on the opening of the wound old linen so that it may draw out that which is of the humors; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that that which is contained within it may flow out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms or trembling of the heart or constriction of the throat, and you see the other good dispositions of him, then know that the wound is safe; and then cure it with cauterizations and with other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore cure that according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision by a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or hand, then treat it with what is prescribed for suture, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if there is damage to the bone, and pieces have been broken off from it, then you should probe the wound and try to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound matures, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is the same as what we have described.
Of the wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment which occurs in the abdomen is either great, small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it is so that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of their replacement immediately, because it opens and swells, making its insertion difficult.
Therefore, a moderate rupture is best, for in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common which are suitable for the suturing of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures specific to the abdomen are also applicable to other wounds, but are nonetheless more suitable for suturing the abdomen.
And I will recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has protruded through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the small size of the rupture as we have said, or because the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil in water squinant, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with this until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it is done with wine, which has astringent properties, and is better than water alone in resolving inflammation.
And so when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the re-entry of it is eased with little effort.
But if its re-entry is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need to worry about dryness and debilitation.
Concerning the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the end is thin, but not as thin as a spatula; rather, it should be somewhat blunt.
And thus, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should make the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant place their compassionate hands between your hands, holding the entire rupture with their hand and adjusting its lips, and then reveal from one to the other whether the suture needs anything else.
And this is the common method of suturing:  first, take a needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then, leaving the width of an annular finger from the edge of the rupture, grasp the needle, being careful not to insert the thread into both ends of the wound simultaneously, but rather use two ends of the thread (ciphac) located below the wound, until you have made it penetrate that part; and you will have joined two ends of the thread and two ends of the ciphac, and thus four ends are made.
Then, tie with a double thread in the loop of the needle, sometimes from two sides together, and then join the two edges of the wound with a proper joining; and then leave the width of a finger and insert another needle in that place, and then tie that with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not cease doing this with needles as necessary until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles injure the flesh too easily; and thick needles are difficult to insert into the wound, therefore they should be of moderate thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be inserted too deeply into both ends of the wound, lest the flesh be too readily injured and the wound reopen before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then, sharpen the ends of the needles so that they do not prick the patient during their sleep; and place on the suture finely powdered material, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those in place until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, because one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient for their closure.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you sew together the four ends of the wound, and the two ends of the lip (or edge), at once, with a needle around which thread is wrapped,  uniform in fineness and thickness.
Then, when you have passed the needle through these four ends, return the needle to the same side from which you began, so that the drawn thread goes from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like the sewing of cloth, by which cloths are strengthened.
And place between each suture the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm, and the intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and moreover, he lived for many years after, and remained in good health.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle that he was, for I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where no ointment or any medicine was found, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper, according to the account of Galen. (Assuming "Paraula" is a misspelling or variant of Galen)
And it is that you begin with the suture of the "codena" (likely referring to a type of stitch or suture technique), and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And then, as the needle penetrates the codena and that same laceration, both straight and along the whole length of the body, leave the end of the thread into which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate the other extremity from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And then, when you have made that penetration, then you make that needle penetrate again in that same extremity of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make the needle penetrate with that penetration with which you make it penetrate the thread at the extremity of the wound which is on his side until all of it penetrates.
And then begin on that same side and sew that with the end of the thread on the other side, and extract the needle from the "codena" which it penetrated, and then return that into that "codena" and sew the end of the thread that is on the other side with that extremity of the wound; and extract that from the "codena" which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time until you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a suture of skins, with which you sew skins, in such a way that you sew the thread once from the side of the extremity of the "codena", and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second type of suture, which is properly called, and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which some physicians sew it, in such a way that they join each part to its corresponding part naturally; and apply the end of one to the other, and the end of the wound to the other end, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle in the extremity of the wound nearest you, on the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that sole [area], and leave two extremities of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in the two extremities of the thread.
And then turn each of them, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other extremities of the wound which are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention.
This is the discourse of Galien (Galen).
And moreover, some say, the suture of the abdomen is rectified with two needles, that is, that you introduce into each of these a thread, and begin with the introduction of the needle from what is nearest you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew leathers similarly.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen.
But the judgment of the medication of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you treat a fresh wound with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and prepare it properly, then place an incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments which are at your disposal until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out, and they are consolidated with cyprus and myrrh; and then treat it as the treatment of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then immediately place on it, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old linen, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you will not need other medication in many situations when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread to the principal members, then it is necessary that you soak linen in olive oil of good quality, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the place which is between the armpits and below the shoulders.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in his intestines, for many times this happens, then cauterize it with warm, black astringent wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound that falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of their magnitude in those, and because of the subtlety of the body of that, and because of the proximity of that to the nature of nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web, and you obtain that recently, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines equally.
But if it has passed to that space and has already become green or black, then that you bind it with a thread over the place that is blackened by it, so that a flow of blood comes, because in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place the two ends of the thread hanging down from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it downwards from the fall of the web and the emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved on you.
A remembrance/reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was ancient and made a fistula, and from it issued a discharge and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to refine it until he was healed and the place was consolidated.
And I, after I saw the place consolidated, feared for the patient that some accident might befall him in his belly; and no ill accident befell him because of this, and he remained in his usual habits eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and was exercised in his movements.
And some experienced ones have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied according to this manner: and it is that you take ants with large heads; and then adjust the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, which has its mouth open, upon the two lips of the wound; then when it bites and grips with its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not dissolve.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease doing this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound.
And then cover that and sew the wound; and those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm will come to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the gut of an animal, since it is thinner than a needle; and it is that you take the end of this gut thread, and it should be very clean, and then tie its end with the thread, wrapped, and then insert that thread into a needle and sew the intestine with it, and then reduce it into the rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a reduction, and not a complete healing.
Concerning the treatment of a fistula.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever member it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call that a fistula.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hardened, white, knotted formation, without pain, which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and therefore it is called a claw-like ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some men it is moist, discharging pus; and very quickly the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is much, and sometimes little; sometimes thick, and sometimes thin.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their cure with medicines, in the division [of the text/treatise].
And it is necessary that I make known to you the cure of a fistula, which is done by means of an operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those that cause damage near a principal member are similar.
Therefore, of these fistulas, the one that is according to this manner is one of the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and to occupy oneself with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and want to reach knowledge of it, then take a probe of horn or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a thin lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent and follows the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has very horrible openings, it is not possible for you to investigate it with a probe.
Then, inject a clyster into the opening, because the moisture by which you inject it will go through the other openings and cleanse them.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula which has many openings, and attend to all this with the utmost of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with your hand on the place, and similar indications and causes incidental to the abscess.
