And when you have considered all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: and it is that you consider then if the fistula is near the surface or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as was said before in the description of it, and remove that which is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the opening of the flesh, or of skin and the like, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it to the extent that is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then administer cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce those into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument (iron).
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not endanger any vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously perform the scraping and cleansing of it with your utmost skill, and then restore it.
And so, if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that the corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which you have not reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but to leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to extract it, then you should extract it with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many pieces of bone, then meticulously perform the extraction of all of them, and of these do not leave anything behind with your skill.
But if some of the moisture causes you difficulty, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will suppurate the wound, and leave it until it reaches the surrounding bone, flesh, and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, and keep the incision that you made in your operation constricted.
Moreover, if you are afraid, apply a linen cloth soaked in crushed herbs with oil, or a linen cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, because when those ligaments suppurate, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is eased.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only on the surface until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to cut out all of that, incising to the point where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, about thirty years of age, who suffered pain in his thigh, and the internal parts were inflamed by matter within the body, until the matter reached the thigh; it was matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and there was no external cause.
And then time passed with this because of the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it; and its cure was delayed until the leg became fistulated, and many orifices formed which all discharged pus and the body's humors.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and they lacked not only wisdom but also skill, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter running from those orifices in great quantity; and the body of that man was quite wasted, and his complexion was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the orifices and found them to extend to all parts of the leg.
And then I carefully incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and found it corrupted, and it was already earthy, corrupted, and blackened; and a part of the leg was corrupted even to the marrow.
And then I sawed off what was exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I believed that in the bone there was only that corruption which I cut away and sawed off, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied a restorative dressing to the wound with consolidative medicine for a long time, and it did not heal.
And then I again probed and uncovered the bone a second time, on top of the first uncovering, and found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed off what appeared to me to be that corruption, and then I was forced to perform the cure and restoration, and it was not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and continued to incise the bone little by little, piece by piece, and was forced to restore it, and it was not restored until I had incised about a palm's breadth of it, and I extracted that with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it healed lightly and was made whole.
And further operation and sectioning of it is not necessary except due to its fragility and its poor tolerance and fear of death, because syncope afflicted him at all hours due to excessive evacuation of humors.
And thus he healed completely, and nothing remained in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in his body was made good and its strength returned; and it was restored to its proper functions and caused him no impediment in walking or harm in any way.
But if a bone protrudes from the body already excised, then you should treat it in this manner:  You should take a ligature and tightly bind it to the extremity of that protruding bone, and order someone to extend it upwards; and place another woolen ligature, thicker than the first, and then bind that over the flesh that is beneath the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board properly under the bone, for when you do this, it is not forbidden that the saw should not cut the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done on the corrupted place little by little, for if the corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear on its surface to the senses, then it is necessary to saw it again.
And if the bone is corrupt but not protruding, with one part continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in part of it, then you uncover the flesh completely on all sides; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continued to the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason but ingenuity (or skill/cleverness).
And if the corruption is in the chest (palm) of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever way appears to you, and you must open it, and clean it according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with every skill it should be rectified for you, when no vein or nerve opposes you.
And know that the incisors and saws for incising these bones are many, according to the location of the bones, and the preparation of those, and the thickness of those, and the subtlety and size and smallness of those, and the hardness and rarity thereof; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you should avail yourself; when with you there is great study and long and detailed knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience with the various types of illnesses, for then you will already have for yourself that which is suitable for the instruments for every illness.
And I am providing to you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will use as simples, with which you will proceed to your prohibition (i.e., treatment or procedure) which you will practice on others. Shape of saw:

Concerning the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
Extremities may be affected either by an intrinsic or extrinsic cause, or if you have treated that corruption with those medicines and the treatment is not effective, or if you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you must incise that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient escapes death or a worse affliction which is a greater impairment of the limb.
And the sign of this affliction is that the limb turns black as if it has been burned by fire, or the blackness spreads until the decay reaches the limb so that it affects the whole body; and then treat it with an incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some venomous creature, such as a sea scorpion, viper, or mad dog, and the like.
And if the corruption or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then you incise the finger and do not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you incise it from the root of the palm, and do not give space to the corruption so that it spreads into the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is certain; and to administer anything else besides this in treatment is futile.
And similarly, with the foot: when corruption affects the toe, then you incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the instep of the foot, then you incise the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him but resignation, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising or cutting the limb is that you tighten a ligature on the part below where you want to incise, and tighten another ligature above the place; and let the assistant extend a ligature to the parts below, and let the assistant extend another ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures, as far as is necessary, until all the flesh is uncovered; and then let it be incised or cut.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient receives a properly adjusted lesion and a hot poultice.
But if a flow of blood occurs while you are working, then carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some styptic powders to it; and then return to your treatment until it is complete.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot from a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe until it affected the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself because he saw the cure going to the limb with such vehemence that he suffered from pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it healed.
For a long time, he experienced that same corruption in the finger of his index hand.
And then he came to me, and I attempted to repel that superfluity with what I applied to his hand in medicine, to clean his body; and the superfluity was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it began in the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, and I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that superfluity, and moreover fearing the incision of his hand might kill him, because the patient's strength was already failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and it was reported to me that that man prepared and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I have added this narrative so that it may be helpful in cases of this illness, and that it may be a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
On the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clysters with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is extended until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus comes out, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin which is over it is like a thin cloth, and it has not reached putrefaction, and does not impinge on the bone, nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
However, if the space is extended so that the corruption affects any member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its treatment is to incise that skin completely as it is rolled up, especially when it is thinned and made like cloth, and make sure that it does not adhere to the place due to its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin adheres and that the corruption has not reached the underlying layers, and there is a widening in the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the arm which is affected has a fetid odor, then you should clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sharp wine and zinc, taking equal parts, and they are mixed in a vessel, and they are cooked over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
And then you take what is necessary of it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the quantity of two hours.
And then extract it with suction; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul smell is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ashes of vine wood, and put water over them; and then filter this and clysterize with it until you are satisfied that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor the lye water, then clysterize it with water and honey with finely ground zinc mixed in, and clysterize it with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, and especially when the wine has a surplus of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and you are satisfied that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that causes the flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize it with some other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster cannula cannot be inserted, then you should widen it little by little with an iron instrument, and place a tent wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment inside until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should approximate its lips with sutures and leave it a size such that the clyster can enter without addition.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is located higher up, then drain it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, because when the pus is retained in the depth of the sinus, flesh will not grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to drain the sinus into the lowest surrounding bone as you wish, then ensure you prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus flows slightly as is expedient to you, and that no pus is retained there.
But the one over the opening of the sinus, change that every hour so that whatever pus is collected there comes out. And so that you know if the pus flowing from it is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter thrown over the opening of the sinus is already adhering, or is about to adhere, then remove it on the third day, and when you unbind the wound, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, because there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are those in whom the opposite is true.
And if undigested pus has come from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dry like diafinicon ointment, when superfluity of addition of zegi is adjusted in it.
And the best remedy with which it is cured, is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and let old wine be poured over it equal in its substance, and mix that with it, and use that.
And it resembles that action, when there is no pus, diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until it thickens, and use that; or that you take myrrh, aloes, and incense, and crush everything and powder it over the honey while it is on the fire, and then spread this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and spread that with the honey which you have cooked until it is thickened, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes aristolochia longa and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and flour of barley and centaury are administered with it, and use those simples or compounded, crush those and sieve them and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use that.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and it already affects the bones or other hard parts or ligaments; then you treat it with fistular treatment, as said above.
Of the treatment of paronychia and worn nails and added finger incision and section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly spreads to the other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its treatment is less valued, or is inflamed from a hot abscess, or is corrupt, and pus forms until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole of it, and quickly its corruption reaches the bone until there is a foul odor from it, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore when you have this with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the superfluity that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound, for a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, now empty, compress the flesh at the edges inside, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin thread under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and lift it up and incise that flesh easily, and place on what remains of the flesh corrosive burning medicines until it is all removed; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already affected the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and remove it, for no healing time is possible while the bone is corrupt.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption going into the finger, then you incise it at one of the joints as its removal dictates, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers percussion or abrasion, and there is vehement pain in it, then it is necessary that you first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse section from above downwards, and be careful that the section does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause the patient vehement pain, and it will be the cause of the generation of superfluous flesh in that place; and then treat that place with what alleviates pain.
But a finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is quickly all fleshy, and quickly has bones, or quickly has nails.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their birth is at some joints of the fingers. But that which is born at some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born at the joints is quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is easy, and it is that you incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the knuckles, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a circular incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then cure it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their knuckles, whether by the birth of a man born thus, or by the consolidation of a wound or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as needed.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to what is prepared and appropriate to the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of vices, they are various.
Vices are various, and they are twisted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and they occur most frequently in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and burden-bearers.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the treatment of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they are incised and that black blood is drawn out, and the other method is that the vein is elevated and entirely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner:  first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, foul blood of the leg is resolved. Then bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to below the knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then, with your hand, press the black blood from the lower part of the leg upwards and from above downwards, until the desired quantity of blood is expelled and the amount you judge sufficient is removed, which will take away the strength of the ailment.
Then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this method: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then place it in a bath and bathe the leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are swollen; or, if a bath is not available, have the limb heated by vigorous exercise.
Then incise the vein along its length, either behind the knee, or at its lowest point below the heel.
Then open the vein with an incision and scrape the vein on all sides until it is sensitive, and it is from its appearance that you will see that redness turning towards blackness.
And then, when you evacuate [blood] from the vein, you will see that which is white like a string.
Then you place a leech below it until it is elevated and is drawn from the vein, and suspend it with a gold or silver hook.
Then make another incision near that incision three fingers' breadth away, and scrape the vein from the part above the vein until it appears.
Then elevate it and incise it at the back of the heel, and then extract it and elevate it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is most superior or highest of all the incisions, until it is all extracted; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you in being extracted and elevated, then introduce a double-pointed, strong needle and tie it and extract it, or introduce a leech below it and move your hand in every direction until it is drawn out. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it and harm has come upon the patient through that injury.
And when it is all expelled, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose wine and un-rose-flavored olive oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the intestine is constricted, having a tortuosity to which there is a twisting in the parts, and is not according to the straightness as we have said, then it is necessary that you incise it in every part of its tortuosity and in the places of its twisting, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it completely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the intestine is cut:
It is not sharpened like hooks, nor is it of sharp extremity so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of a thick, flexible nature, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein through its thinness;  it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of a vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little moisture, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, maggots, and worms are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is that vehement inflammation occurs in the leg; and the area is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of that blister, which resembles a plant root.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachm to two drachms, and tie it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as you see something emerge from it externally, wrap that in lead and tie it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap up the remainder; and do not incise the root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the area and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein, in some men, a growth emerges that is five palms and fingers in length, and it has even reached me that one emerged that was twenty palms long.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long channel into the body, until everything that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the area for some days with medicines; and then treat it with the cure for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch emerges from each orifice.
And therefore cure it in the way we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because many times it happens to cows, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes throughout the body, rising and falling, and is shown by the feeling of its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and snakes and earthworms and ascarids are produced in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is not done except according to its movement and appearance to the touch, and then it is necessary that you tightly bind that which is above it and that which is below it with strong ligature and constriction, and then dry it above and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you place a cautery with fire on the place until it dies, and the greatest cause that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong constriction, and then dry above it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard against foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision (or cutting) for the illness nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some members, and then is moved from member to member.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that swelling move like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than a worm, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I realised that it was moving throughout her body, as I had seen; and I was very surprised by its change of place and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find that pain is transferred from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my own eyes in the same way as in this woman.
And I do not think this is so, except that it is because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this case that wind-like sensation appeared to be transferred.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this backward cure in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that place and slightly dry it  until that constricted wind is expelled, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then you treat it with chest percussion and with things that expel winds, and drive them out with foul-smelling pills and pills of Serapion and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they land, for their diversity is such that there are those with large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and those that have three angles, and those that have barbs; and those that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, it is when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and comes out of the skull which is over the brain, for it causes sudden vehement pain, and scotoma (blurred vision), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile, and speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and a white humidity resembling pus comes from the wound, and the eyes seem watery like the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside [the body].
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, is that it is near the left breast, and one feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not in something empty; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death follows without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus: the expulsion of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels which are near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cool air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm which is in the chest, the pain then is near the small ribs, and the breath is drawn with great and vehement pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are paralyzed.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is fixed there, there will be some discharge of pus from the wound, or the flesh, or intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope for its cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine issues forth, and something proceeds from it to the outside parts, and very quickly pain overcomes the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the greater dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may thereby understand your treatment.
Which is the reason why, in a man, an arrow fell into the lacrimal duct of his eye at the root of the nose, and I extracted it from him on the other side, below the lobe of his ear, and he is healed, and no harm happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into him in the lobe of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I found only a small point with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with an iron head in which there were no barbs.
And the Jew is healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this is one which has barbs; and then I incised over it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I worked delicately with it, and I extracted it, and the Christian is healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and then, after thirty days had passed and no change had occurred in his condition, I incised over the arrow and pulled it out, and he is healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow fell into his back, and the wound consolidated on him, and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow fell into her belly, and the wound is healed within her, and nothing is changed in her condition, and the woman finds no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound is healed and the arrow broken, finding here little pain, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the king's princes that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure about three days after the arrow's impact.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I investigated it with a thin probe, and I did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that that prickling was from the tip of the arrow.
And I plastered the area with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the area would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could cut into it.
And no significant incident occurred in the area indicating that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no incident occurred; and the wound was meanwhile consolidated, and the patient rested from its extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I tested it, and I found the thin tip of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I applied to the opening of the wound that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and I spent time with this for a period of about four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to subtly and skillfully work on it with ingenious methods, until I managed one day with ingenious instruments to grasp it with suitably made forceps, as the form of these will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored that wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound was consolidated, and the patient was healed completely, and he suffered no ill effects.
And I recount to you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows so that you may consider this a rule and explanation of what I have not said, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not comprehended in narration, nor do books contain them.
And skilled artisans compare the small with the large; and what is present with what is absent; and find new operations and new instruments due to the consideration of unusual situations in the art when they occur.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the limbs into which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by counter-traction from the opposite side.
And that which is removed from the place where it entered, is an arrow situated in fleshy tissue, and is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to removal at the time of discovery, then you must leave it for some days until the flesh around it softens, and its removal and extraction are eased.
And similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not yield to removal, then leave it for some days and repeatedly attempt its removal with traction and movement each day, and it will eventually be extracted.
And if it still does not yield to removal after some days, then you must perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a fine drill until the space around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is beyond feeling, then you should search for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then draw it out with instruments suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the probe, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should incise the wound until it is widened and it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held by barbs, then you should carefully free the flesh in which it is embedded by every means you can; and work on this, if you cannot otherwise remove the flesh by breaking off the barbs and twisting them until they yield.
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting motions of your hand and forceps on all sides until it yields; and proceed with the utmost delicacy so that the arrow is not broken and that its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then attempt it again, for it will then be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then administer the treatment described in the appropriate chapter.
And be careful not to cut any veins, nerves, or tendons, and use every possible means to extract the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And so that you may contract the wound so that the patient is positioned according to the location of the arrow's entry, because that is more beneficial. But if this is not possible for you, then administer what you can according to the circumstances.
But if arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of the arrow projects outside the body, or if the tip of the arrow is curved, feeling the part above the aforementioned area and seeing it prominent; then you should cut above it; and the incision should be according to the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will be easily removed in this manner.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then you twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrow breaks in the bone, and widen the wound itself, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is still inside, then you push it all the way through.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer impulsion, then you introduce, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity of it enters the base of the arrow and then push it through.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then you push it through with a strong instrument entering it, because the arrowhead is eased by it.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow falls into the foot, or into the belly, or into the bladder, or into the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut around it, then cut around it and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the forceps with which arrows are pulled out:
Let the ends of them be like the beak of a bird, which are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the tightness of it.
like a ray, so that the entry of it into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins that are in the two apparent temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called *aspicientz*; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the tip of the nose; and two *guidegi* that are at the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called *algelic* —; and there are two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these, one is the cephalic, and it is on the outer side of the arm; and the people call it "the vein of the head", and it is a superficial vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people call that one "vein of the whole body", and the basilic is the vein which is situated on the inner side of the arm, and is called "citillar", and the common people call that one "vein of the belly"; and the median vein of the arm, and it is situated above the elbow, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and the salvatella, and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger, and there are two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is that which is below the bend of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those.
The opening of the veins that are near the ears is for chronic catarrhs and migraines, and headaches, and old, bad ulcers of the head.
And the manner of bleeding those veins is as I have recounted to you:  it is that the head of the patient should be raised, and the head of the patient should be rubbed at the occiput in the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should constrict his neck with a band until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed places of the head; and feel them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those with your finger, then bleed there with a lancet.
Then you take the lancet knife, and it is that which is sharp, and then you insert that below the vein into the skin until the blood comes to the opening. Then raise your hand with the vein and the skin to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers placed together, or thereabouts; and let blood flow out as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already and previously in the beginning of the book [I have described] the incision of these and their cauterization.
But of the two arterial veins that are at the temples, the benefit of phlebotomy is for chronic and severe migraine, and persistent ophthalmia, and a flow of acute superfluities effused into the eyes.
And the manner of phlebotomizing these is as I recounted to you: constrain the patient's neck very much until the two veins appear to the senses by manifest appearance and their pulse is demonstrated beneath your finger; and then you mark with a probe.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper parts with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet below, and raise the vein upwards; and incise it just as you did in the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your necessity.
And then loosen the constriction of the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it above with strong constriction, and leave it until it is healed.
And already the remembrance of them, and of their incision, and the drawing [of blood] has been preceded in the beginning of the book.
Therefore, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein in old diseases of the face, and for migraine, rigor, and fetid ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recounted to you; that is, that the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a fossor, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fossor upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or other such thing in the manner thereof, and let the blood flow according to the quantity which you desire; and then unbind the perforation of the patient and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of acute extremity as are the other phlebotomies, rather it should be of a blunt extremity, and phlebotomize it across, for the bone is near and would easily break there if the phlebotomy were fine/sharp.
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the lacrimal ducts of the two eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for redness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize those veins, and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across rather than lengthwise, with a small phlebotomy instrument, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the phlebotomy instrument is sharp, its point will very easily be broken.
And then make the blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton there, and bind it for one night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and vehement headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and redness that occurs in the face, and especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient should tighten his neck, and then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin phlebotomy instrument and insert it into the middle of the bridge of the nose in a straight line, because the vein is not apparent to the touch there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
Carefully move your hand with the phlebotomy instrument little by little and make the blood flow according to your need; and then bind it for a night, for it is lightly restored (or healed).
But the application of phlebotomy to the two temples is beneficial for the constriction of the throat, and for the beginning of leprosy, and for many diseases, and impetigo, and bad, corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of phlebotomy in these (temples) is that the patient should hold each temple with a bandage; and the surgeon should stand over the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a stool.
And then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide phlebotomy instrument little by little; and then extract blood according to a moderate quantity or according to what necessity demands; and then do likewise with the other vein.
And then untie the bandage and bind the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning because the wound is healed.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the alveolar veins is good for small abscesses and pustules of the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and bad ulcers and fissures, and bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.
And the manner of phlebotomy of those (veins) is that you make the patient sit before you, and bind their neck with a bandage, and then you pull back their lips and look for two veins, of which one is on the right of the lip and the second on the left; and the identification of each is by the blackness of each of the two, and this is more so because in the area of these there are small black veins; and then incise each with an incisive incision.

Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping vessels are of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cupping vessels that are administered with scarification and bloodletting, there are fourteen places on the body, of which one where cupping vessels are placed is at the nape of the neck, which is the back part of the head; and the middle place of the two sides of the neck together; and cupping vessels of the chin below the jaw below the mouth; and cupping vessels of the two shoulder blades; and cupping vessels of the loins, and that is above the end of the buttocks; and cupping vessels of the two flanks, and they are of the two sides; and cupping vessels of the two hips; and cupping vessels of the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the strength through them, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping because of illness or a continuing condition, let it be administered at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at any time whatsoever.
Therefore, when you see any of those conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied, it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is sunken in the eyes; but this should be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on someone of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a one.
And therefore, it is not advisable to use it on the elderly, and on those whose heads suffer from cold ailments.
But to the one on whom this is continuously applied, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore you should instruct the cupper to place his hand a little below the cupping glass, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alkehel (likely referring to a specific substance or technique) is in the place of phlebotomy of the dark vein and the basilic vein: and therefore it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the breath, and the splitting of the instrument of breath (possibly referring to the lungs or trachea), and cough, and repletion (likely referring to excess fluids or congestion).
And it is necessary that the alkehel cupping glass be slightly elevated, for if it is placed too low, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with alhagdahan (likely another specific substance or technique) benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia (eye inflammation), and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand with scarification, lest he cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar oral diseases, and it is in place of phlebotomy of the  alicheric (likely a specific vein near the lips), which is in the two lips.
But cupping that is done on the two shoulder blades benefits caphatan (likely a specific condition), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two inner parts of the ankles benefits similarly to phlebotomy of three veins, that is, of the basilic, median cubital (fusca - possibly a variant or less precise term for it), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too much with scarification, because the place is devoid of flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups on the hips, and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that it should be a large cupping glass, and that it should be of brass, because the place requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are quickly broken and should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs diminishes pain by manifest depletion, because it draws blood from the whole body, and benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits dropsy and swelling; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two ankles.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause syncope in many men.
And of the ankle cuppings, the benefit is similar to the benefit of the leg cuppings.
And the quality of the application of cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw it by tempered suction, and do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors may come to the place by equal admixture. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the blood's redness appear.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and consider the disposition of the bodies.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification and make it somewhat deeper, and temper the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and its humidity. But in the second [scarification], to the scrutiny acquired by the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the end.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term scarification in which there is only the depth of the [finger]nail.
And if the one whose nail is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for the cupping glasses be anointed with lenitive, aperitive, and resolvent ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balm mint oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar ones.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be prohibited, and for such a one the places for cupping should be washed before cupping with rose water, or with cold water of solanum (nightshade), or with poppy water, or with  borage water, and similar ones.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash his cupping sites with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for one whose superfluities are thick, wash the cupping sites with old wine, or with water of sanc (perhaps a type of herb, possibly savory or majoram), which is marjoram, or a decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before a bath; rather, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and he should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the person to be cupped or bled ought to be regulated [before cupping]?  or  What must be done to prepare the patient for cupping or bloodletting?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning:  if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive in sour wine and lettuce, and succory, and julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as poultry, and the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with sour things, and similar things.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of succory seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine, milder between old and new; and that he be advised to use little drink and food, and give him foods such as poultry, hempseed, peas, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting his drink be greater than his food.
And it is necessary that at certain hours some men be given theriac alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or theriac before cupping or before bloodletting or nearby, so that the principal members are strengthened and the blood is thinned; and they should not take those things that warm.
But cupping without scarification should be applied over the liver, and over the spleen and gout, and over the navel, in the areas of the knees, and the groin, because these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
And it is not desired for those [members] except the attraction of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of nasal bleeding; and we use this so that cold, invisible windiness in the member is dissolved, just as we place it on the belly and navel; for this rarefies the member and warms it and reduces the pain because it resolves windiness.
And it is placed on the throat when inflammation or swelling occurs there, for by virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the inflammation and removes the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of windiness enclosed in them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, with warm water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of administering a cupping glass with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and let the length of the horn be half a palm; and let it be at the side of it, in the place that is in the circumference, half of it, the hole being small enough for a needle to enter it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or citrus, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb because of its placement on it.
And let there be a transverse bar of bronze or iron in the middle of it, on which a candle is placed with the fire.
And now let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for young people and those with large bodies.
And the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with a decently sized wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the lower part so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole we have mentioned, until when we have the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be removed.
And then ignite the wick according to its manner, and repeat this if you need to.
But in cupping, which is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and is not administered unless it is filled with water, and is placed on the limb alone.

Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs in which the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their small size, such as the finger and gum and similar; or to a member devoid of flesh, such as the finger and nose and similar.
And the quality of using those:  If you have found leeches that are in clean fresh waters of a spring.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no matter remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then let them be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to suck the area with a cupping glass, it is afterwards more beneficial.
And if not, then wash the area with wine vinegar and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the attachment of the leeches, and it is oozing, then you dip a cloth in cold water and place that over it until the oozing is stopped.
And if the blood is profuse, then sprinkle on it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and things similar to them of styptic qualities, until the bleeding is stopped; or let halves of roasted beans be placed on the area, and let them remain until the beans stick to the place, and the bleeding is stopped.
And it is necessary, if needed, to reapply leeches, when it is possible to change those.
And if the leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then let them be applied, because when they sense any blood they immediately attach.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle on them a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ash, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish and vulgar physicians boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter of them.
And for this reason, this manner of knowledge is kept private in our land; and I have never forgotten this in any way; and I have not acquired this except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their understanding, until I extracted their knowledge.
And then I added experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and on which my experience is based; since I have shortened it for you and freed it from lengthy occupation; and I have shortened it for the sake of brevity; and I have shown it for the sake of clarity; and I have presented many forms of the instruments that are used in it, as is done by way of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter one.
Summaries and collections of bone fractures, of those things which are necessary to know beforehand, before we begin the discussion of broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to the understanding of which, in the beginning, the craftsman is compelled to stand firm on the truth of them; that is, you and those who are ready to know or learn this noble art, as well as others of you.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you should initially attend to it with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the person who has suffered this is an infant, or an elderly person in a weakened state, or if the time is one of intense heat or intense cold.
And moreover, let him be content with a light diet of herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit rich meats and overfilling oneself with food and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a benign abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his former regimen, to which he is accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the patient be fed nourishing foods, foods that are very substantial and heavy, and in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat boiled in water, and rice, and heads [of grain?], and peas, and cow's stomachs, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things. With this regimen, the consolidation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their first youth and in the elderly, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature fits and brings together to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are together conjoined and bound together, until they come to the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until nothing prevents it from its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that you provide the patient with foods in which there is weight, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another.
And I come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Much diversity of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arcuate fracture, besides those that happen in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and not proceeding, just as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type belongs its own proper treatment or torment in its restoration, as recollection of it will be recounted distinctly in its own place.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this is a bone fracture known: its crookedness, and the eminence and appearance of it to the touch, and that of it when we compress it with the hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is anything felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Perhaps there is a crookedness or small fissure; then you should not move it with your hand and with complete compression; rather, apply to it the medicines of which mention will be made in their appropriate place; and it should also be tightly bound with a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is bent but not split in two, or other such accident has not befallen it, as if the whole part is removed from its comparison, then you should begin at once to rectify it before an abscess happens to it by chance.
And if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days until the abscess ceases; and then treat it according to whatever method you can, gently and skillfully.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a more serious injury has occurred.
And then bind it as the remedy will indicate.
But if the bone is injured, then extension of that broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords, or with one cord and your hand.
And your position should be such that you place the injured limb according to its natural shape, while the broken parts of the limb are extended; then you must force those parts back into their places skillfully and in whatever way you can.
And be careful, according to your strength, that you do not cause pain or injury to the patient through your operation; and endeavor to the best of your ability to apply one bone to its fellow, according to their best possible alignment.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch it with your hand, and if you see anything amiss, correct it and align it to the extent of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause hot abscesses through their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen through their operations many times.
And the nature of the binding of the quieted limb is that which I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their largeness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the forearm and similar to those, then the wrapping should be of very fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, the wrapping should be broad and firm, because a broad bandage connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with even pressure, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, then your equalization evacuation, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the fracture site. And then extend yourself with the wrapping of the fracture site a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you take a large part of the site.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken area with a bandage, and then come with the bandages to the lowest part of the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the bandage and in the softening of it as we have said in the first bandage above.
And then place between the layers of the bandage linen or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then bind over these bandages well-made splints immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area, and then place over it that which dries the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave that for some days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be of half a reed of smooth, well-shaped wood; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to how it fits the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with string properly as we have said of tightening, and it is so that your tightening is according to the place of the fracture more, and the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools placing their threads of twisted rope, and it is a great error, when the tightening loses equality.
And similarly, very fine threads are not convenient, because you will not achieve with those what you want by tightening, and it is not necessary that between splint and splint there should be less than a finger's width.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those some carded flax or wool until it does not cause him harm.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, and I will return to this in the following chapter according to its particularities.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason is that, when the limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints on it until about five or seven days, or more, according to your certainty of avoiding a hot abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients made restorations, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
Method of a common plaster, a bruised remedy for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many constitutions and especially for women and children, because it is something that neither heat nor cold overcomes: and it is that you take powdered moly, and this is the marrow of the flour that hangs from the movement of the mill on the walls of the mill.
And make it thus as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white, and make the confection of it neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and meligrane, which is the root of wild pomegranate, of each one dram; of myrrh and aloe, five drams; of aloes, twenty drams; all should be crushed and sifted and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores lightly broken bones, and benefits many men.
Another method of plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take of meligrane and white mallow root, ten drams; of myrrh and of aloe, five drams; of acacia, six drams; of Armenian bole, twenty drams.
All should be crushed in a mortar with much grinding, and be sifted, and be mixed with water or with egg white and be administered.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let dirty wool be taken and be submerged in sour wine and boiled oil and be placed on the area.
But in this plaster there is no restorative virtue, it only draws out hot abscesses and expels pain.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of the fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; crush everything and make a plaster from them.
Method of a brief plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remainder of an abscess: take roots of mallow, of chamomile, and violet flowers, of pea flour, of all an equal part; let all be crushed and be mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix that with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
