Another method of a stronger plaster in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of the limb: take roots of mallow and linseed, and of pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all in equal parts; let all be crushed and be mixed with willow water or with sweet water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
But Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium consistency between thick and thin.
But in Gaul it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the things which cause desiccation with little heat are thus, like myrrh, incense, and similar to those things that we have mentioned.
Method of a plaster that benefits lesions and pain: take millet, wax, shaved human hair or bird down, and mallow, and salt, in equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and when it should be loosened is this: consider if no pain or itching has occurred, and the bone is not broken or dislocated; then you do not loosen it for many days. And if vehement pain, itching, or inflammation occurs at the location, then immediately loosen or untie it at that time, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a damp sea sponge, and immerse it in lukewarm water, and with this wash the area until that itching ceases and the pain is calmed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place on it dirty wool soaked in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided, and its pain is lessened.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster and do not tighten it as much as your first constriction, but subtly with it until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, the redness, the pain, the inflammation, and all the symptoms are now completely subsided, and you judge there is no need for plaster or binding, then you repeat it as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not untie it until three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days depending on what appears to you upon loosening the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the binding more than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as is preceded by their recovery.
But if you see the site of the fracture drying and thinning more than is appropriate, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you untie it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set properly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their operation, and a great danger; and if it were right, the Ancients would have enlarged upon this in their books and would have worked with it.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that you do not do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a sword, and the blow breaks all the bone up to the marrow, which is beneath the bone, just as it happens in a tree trunk, and therefore this type is called *ascial* (axial); or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its flesh but the incision does not go through to the other side; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (complete elevation/separation).
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and there is fracture which is a contusion or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or a fall on stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, so that it approaches the periosteum which is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone, as fine as a small hair, and therefore this type is called a hairline fracture.
And there is a fracture which is caused either by a fall or by a blow with a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to be driven inwards, and it is made in a concave place, just as happens to the plates of earthenware when a blow is struck and part of them is driven inwards.
And this happens more often in the bones of humid bodies, such as the skulls of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will bring everything back to its place with repositioning.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by touching them and by investigating them with probes, and by the presence of corrupt flesh that is over them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is discovered on the bone, and it is wiped clean, and a probe is run over it, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider at the beginning the patient's symptoms, and then if you see by the symptoms that it signifies by manifest signs, such as vomiting of bile, extension (possibly referring to limb extension or prostration), loss of consciousness, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, and swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar symptoms in the major conditions, without a doubt it is not healed (or, it will not heal).
And if you see no unusual symptoms and you expect recovery, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person has come to you at the beginning as injured, and this is in the winter days, then you must study the state of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must endeavor to set the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue), and before the symptoms that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and is with small pieces and attrition (grinding or wearing away), then you must incise the comminuted and atrite part, as I will explain to you.
And you shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as best you can according to the nature of the injury, and that it be gentle on the patient.
And if you find, upon examination of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary, and that is to fill the area with cloths soaked in rose-colored wine and oil, and allow the injury to rest until the abscess is sedated (reduced), and you are sure of the cessation of bleeding; and then begin with the encirclement of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its form:  And then apply another incisor like this one, a little wider than that; and this is its form:
And then apply yet another, wider incisor than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and let them be sharp at the tips of their ends, and let them be made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them easily in percussion on the incisor so that you do not strike the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you apply the incisors with the drills, which are called non-penetrating drills.
And they are not called thus except because these do not reach the end of the skull bone to that which is near it, because the drill has a round end or edge on that which is below its sharp head, similar to an edge or a small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a great number, each of which is suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have present in all parts of the skull a tip which is sharp, the quantity in length and shortness being according to the quantity of thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; and then move the drill to another place, and place [the drills] so that the space between each hole is the size of the radius or thereabouts.
And then incise with incisors that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as possible in order to lighten it, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument which you will have prepared for this, such as small pincers and forceps.
And it is necessary that you take great care that the incisor does not touch any part of the brain.
And so when you have encircled the bone by perforating it, and it is separated from the brain if it is connected to it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and smooth its roughness from that which is on the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but however it must be thinner and finer than the other incisors.
And if some small and sharp fragments of bone remain, then take them away easily with whichever instrument you have available; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier, far from frightening, which Gualia describes and praises highly; and this is his statement: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the tip of this slow and gentle incisor of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided into two edges lengthwise, like a knife; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the brain with its surface, the sharp part cutting into the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until you cut through all the bone easily as it is turned, and you are sure that the membrane will not suffer any accident that you fear in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
Then, if any matter attached to the bone remains in the membrane in any part of the bone, cut it away with the lenticular end of that incisor, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the membrane, and what is cut away or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughness and fine splinters, then you must scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, for which you have prepared a number, of various sizes so that it is possible for you to use in each place the most suitable and most appropriate of them according to what guides your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must proceed in your scraping, with which you scrape the bone, beginning with the broadest of those rasps, and then use progressively the more subtle ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you arrive at using the finest and most subtle of all those rasps.
But in each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what guides you to their rectification.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And we have shown this which we have written above in the case of large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you scrape the bone, then you must take a cloth of linen according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place it over the thin cloth, doing this as gently as possible so as not to burden the patient; and then apply a linen bandage over this, but do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound in place alone.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a warm abscess; and then unbind it and then administer medicines which counter excessive dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour, powdered lentils, and birthwort, and similar things.
And it is so that you make a powder of these medicines which is sprinkled over the wound, once they are dry; and universally, administer in this situation any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite (i.e., cause further irritation).
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clean; and that there be no filth of ointment or other matter; and do not allow virulence to increase in it; for when it is applied to the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and causes grave harm to the patient.
And it affects the patient's head, as the bone is cleaned and especially when its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if the patient suffers the consequences we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly pernicious.
And if there is blackness not from the medicine that is placed on top, but it is by virtue of that medicine that causes that blackness, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these things moisten a cloth, and then place it on the head; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of a broken nose.
Know that a broken nose is not broken in two parts together, but sometimes only one, because it has two bones; because the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, and it suffers only abrasion, crookedness, and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into his nose, and align that fracture inside with your index finger, and with your thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and take care that your operation does not cause the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be set with the end of a thin rod.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same.
And strive for his restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
And then insert into the end of the nose a rolled-up piece of linen, if the fracture is in one part, or two small rolls, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the roll be of such a size as to fill the opening of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the rolls should be dipped in a mixture (boder), and changed every day, but I have not seen this; rather it is necessary that the roll be soaked in egg white mixed with fine powder; and then leave the roll until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a roll, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, is inserted into the nose; and let this be done if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, make rolls.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, plaster the nose with cerate or cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it externally with sesame flour and powdered chickpeas mixed with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary that you make an incision over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then sew up the incision, and treat it with what causes granulation and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary that you treat it with rolls and administer lead canulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with that the hump of the fracture inwards, towards the outer parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; thus properly setting it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from both sides according to the straightness until its alignment is possible.
And if concussion of teeth with disruption occurs, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place over the broken jaw a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place on the cloth a suitably made large compress, or a piece of sole equal to the length of the jaw; and then bind that above according to how the binding is prepared for you and as the application requires, until it is healed.
And instruct the patient to use rest and quiet, and give him food of easily digestible lions. (The word "leons" here is likely a mistake and should probably be something like "liquids" or "broths".  The text seems to be implying a liquid or soft diet.)
And if you estimate that the shape is altered in any way, then you should look at it and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify what is altered and apply a plaster with finely ground powder, with egg white, or with starch, after you have removed the cerate, and place soft wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and adjusted over it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not unbind it until it is healed and the fracture is firm; because many times this fracture is firm in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said in its sedation until it is healed, and the abscess is resolved.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for removing them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a phlebotomy lancet according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for this and will promote granulation, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is fractured.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part, above the muscle, and this fracture happens in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore; or it is such that splinters are present, and it is difficult to restore; or it is such that the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the splint and supports the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then bind the fracture with your bandages until its shape is made as needed, and there is neither a small prominence nor a concavity.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the armpit of the patient a pad of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand until you bind the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it has reached the sternum, then the patient should be supine on their neck, and you should place a moderately sized cushion on their muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle that is on the sternum above is elevated, and then you straighten the fracture and bind it with your bandages.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bad, then you should make an incision over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should carefully incise it with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves as a splint; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size or smallness of the bone.
But let its length be as long as is possible for you in the operation; that it has two ends like a spoon, and one end is wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you should approximate the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And ensure that the bandage is not loosened over the broken bone; and for this reason you should see the patient every day, so that when the ligament is softened or you see the bandage is loosened, you can then rectify this and tighten the bandage.
And then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their testicle at night; so that with this, their side is elevated from that, and therefore the fracture of the clavicle is elevated from the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless an abscess or swelling occurs at the site, before twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the reduction and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle is restored and coagulated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the situation, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken on its lateral side, and not shattered, only the margins [are broken].
And so when the place [the shoulder blade] is broken—and it is broken in the middle of the place—and is not known except by touch, then, according to what the form of the fracture is, forcibly prepare it and reduce it to its natural form in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area a powder made from finely ground egg white and lion's wool; then place a poultice of doubled cloth; then place over it a smooth, finely woven bandage, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage is concave in any place on the shoulder blade, then you should fill that concavity with lion's wool, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
Then bind it above with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the bandage, until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time that it is loosened, bandage it again and tighten it, and straighten the bandage if it has shifted from its place.
And lay the patient on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And then when this number is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and you should know for certain that this is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the fractured bone and places itself below the clavicle, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle concerning the cauterization of an abscess if it occurs there.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a foot fracture.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster because they are more compact and less fragmented.
And among the accidents [complications] when the middle of the foot is broken, it inclines inward, and great pain occurs, as well as difficulty breathing, coughing, a strong pulse, and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from observation/feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be placed supine on his back, with a cushion placed between his shoulders; then gently press on his muscles, and adjust the broken bones with hands from both sides, and be meticulous in this operation and in the setting, in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone is restored to its proper shape.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place over that a splint of thin willow or alder wood, or something similar in lightness, after wrapping it in cloth; and carefully bind this over the broken bone so that it is not moved; and use a bandage, round in shape, on the dorsal side sometimes, and tighten it with a decent firmness;  and regularly check the bandage, and tighten it when it loosens.
And if necessity demands loosening of the bandage because of corrosion, itching, pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should examine and loosen it, remove the plaster, and correct what has happened with appropriate treatment; and then reapply the plaster if it is appropriate; and adjust the tightness until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs occurs only in the thicker parts, which are two; and in the extremities, at the front part,  no such crushing occurs because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from perception because of its investigation with the fingers.
And the restoration should be such that you align the fracture with your fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as it should be, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if that is needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and he experiences difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in that with many contrivances.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we avoid this so that it does not easily cause a hot abscess if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it helps with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw with force; and this is similar to reasoning, but however, it is feared that the cupping glass will attract superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is even as it envelops that according to roundness; and moreover, the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy with food and medicine.
And if a vehement cause constricts the patient which he does not suffer, and the bone pushes the membrane with a piercing injury,  we fear for the patient, then it is necessary that we make an incision on the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place on it an instrument that preserves the *ciphac* (likely a misspelling or archaic term, possibly referring to a type of splint or bandage), the recollection of which is previously mentioned, and that we incise the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and we treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess forms inside, then you should take and mix powders in rose oil and place it on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him be supine on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Chapter 9: On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and then comes their attrition, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider, for if you see his hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, extend or flex them, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most dispositions it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if he shows in the vertebrae of the neck the same thing and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then observe his feet.
Then if you see that they are softened, the same thing that happened in the hands occurs in the feet, and also when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he is supine on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not work on his cure.
But if none of these things happen, the cause is better and lighter.
And the cure for him is that you should apply to the suppuration of the hot abscess,  which you place on the affected vertebra,  pure rose oil alone, or with boiled egg yolks; apply that there several times a day until the hot abscess is seated/reduced.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he not sleep on the side where he finds pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that because of the friction of the bone splinters or some cause it has separated from it, then you must cut it at the base and remove that bone; and then join the two edges of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the posterior part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the hip joint, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum, and reposition the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible according to the second equalization; and then apply a plaster and splint, if you need them, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel broken splinters there, then you should remove them, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are, the fracture is only a separation at their extremities and is lengthwise, sloping inwards; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is its shape.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you align that according to what is prepared for you by equal alignment until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or slopes downwards, then you have the patient lie on their stomach so that the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And then when you have aligned it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or reed over that, and bind it tightly so that you do not fear a puncture of the fracture or displacement of the splint.
And fill the cavity with something that fills it until it takes the binding evenly, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is settled, and place on it something that settles it as has been previously described; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if splinters are found in the bone or it is comminuted (shattered), then you remove them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone (clavicle).
The collarbone (clavicle) is that which is between the side and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at the two extremities, and then it should be suspended from a raised position.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then place his broken arm over the splint until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which the curve of the splint should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy object from it, or the attendant should extend it downwards; and then the physician should set the fracture with his hands, until the fracture returns to its proper place.
And the other method is that the patient should be lying supine on his back and suspend the arm from his neck with a bandage; and then command two assistants that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the lower part; and each of them should extend to his part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tighten it over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants should extend to their two parts.
And so if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the armpit and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that place and over that elbow.
Equalize that with ease and without effort, and then when it is equalized as needed and is applied with good application, then tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the constriction until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten that.
And the way to tighten that is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand over its muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is so that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is so that your bandage over the fracture site should be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you proceed.
And if you fear a festering abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that no itching or inflammation occurs in the area; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the pressure of the tight bandage; and then rectify all that according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the injured area a poultice full of well-carded wool, and check every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or distorted, or that the bandage is not softened; then rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient's food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until he begins to recover strength; and then it is appropriate that you give him thicker food.
Because it is customary for the injured limb and leg to be bandaged for forty days; and then they should be unbound and a bath should be administered and they should be treated with ointments that rectify this.
And if the fracture is severely damaged, then you should not untie the bandage and splints until after ten days or even two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger focil is broken alone, or the smaller, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the focil is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small focil above, then it is convenient for the physician, before its restoration, that he applies slight extension easily until it is aligned.
But if the large focil is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply a more forceful extension.
And if both focils are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the figure of your hand during its restoration and its extended extension on a cushion, and that the pulse of the hand be in the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the auricular finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be lying on a squared pillow.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer from exertion.
Then the minister should extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another minister should extend it above similarly.
And then the physician should fit the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if the fracture of the bone is jagged, then you must forcefully reduce all the pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated jagged piece appears in it, and you have no hope of its restoration, then you should cut it off and remove it according to the manner which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dedicated a separate chapter to this, and therefore take the treatment from that.
And if an abscess occurs near the beginning of your treatment, then you should line a cloth with a plaster made of rose oil and white wax, of a consistency between thick and thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess has subsided; and then remove the plaster and apply a poultice prepared from crushed molli with egg white, and then apply a compress; and let the compress which is placed over the fracture be thicker and firmer.
And know that the number of compresses on the arm is varied according to the major conditions, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your binding over the fracture site firmly and tightly, and if the binding is closer to the upper parts of the fracture or to the lower parts of it, apply the binding a little more loosely, as was mentioned earlier at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, and not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be of a consistency midway between thin and thick, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any complication arises requiring correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the binding.
And if the fracture is now softened and the bone is reset and similar to the others, then correct all this with your skill.
And further consider whether nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the binding, for then it is necessary to loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient experiences none of the complications we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended by its own agitated movements, and make it sleep on its back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or even quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the strength of the individual.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the wrist and finger joints; instead, they often suffer from attrition.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down, and a cushion should be placed beneath him to ensure even support, and then place his hand on the extended cushion.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should align them until they are properly joined.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess is present; and then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and wrap it in linen.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm, then make a kind of cloth ball, and instruct the patient to hold it with his fractured palm; then it should be bound tightly with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather, with cloth in which it is wrapped, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a bandage above and another below in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, in the manner that the hand is bound to the splint between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the finger knuckles, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as necessary and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be bandaged and bound to the finger next to it; or all should be bound together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and in addition to that, the disposition of hot abscesses and oppose — with your operation — what is necessary when any of these things occur, according to the repeated description of them.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is felt according to whether it is broken at the front or the back part.
And its restoration is done in this way: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the physician stretches the entire bandage to its extent, according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the bandage should be close to the knee, on the side of the kneecap, so that the extension happens upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then the bandage should be placed close to the knee, and the extension should be to the parts below.
And then the physician joins the bone with both his hands until he reduces it according to its natural shape and the bone is joined by a good join; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess is reduced, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made like this:  You should wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long strip of material between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the strip come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh that which is superfluous from the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is firm.
And then begin with a firm bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and the farther you move away from the fracture site, the less tight, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the strip to the lower parts until they reach the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not dislodged from its place.
And then leave the bandage on it, so that it does not cause corrosion, hot abscess, swelling, or anything similar to these in the limb; and if any of these things occur, then loosen it, and correct what has happened immediately, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that protrudes, then you must replace it as best you can; and if not, dress it and keep it in place, and treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it heals.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is tightened further after splints have been applied, just as we have said in the case of the arm.
However, in the restoration made in this way, the patient does not experience lameness; and if only one leg is restored, without the other leg being applied to it, then it is necessary that he will limp on the one that is always affected.
And know that the thigh is bandaged for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but it often causes attrition (or wearing away).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or break in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and above all it is accompanied by pain.
And its restoration is such that what is broken of its parts is joined together with said splints until it is united and conjoined as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication (or setting).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and bind it above with a bandage suitable for this.
And then consider all the preparations that we have recommended in other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that all accidents are countered with what is convenient for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large and is named by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and it is named fibula.
And the same kinds of fractures happen to these two bones of the leg as happen to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and its operation is one and the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is deformed in all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is deformed in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find that below, and it is among those things not easily seen by you.
Then administer extension, setting, and bandaging of the splints according to that same method.
However, if the leg fracture is large, having many splinters, the extension should be less and lighter, and alleviate it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is a certain operation similar to that on the arm, and when you apply the splints and complete your operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood, which are placed under the parts which are placed on the soles, and are made equally from palm branches; choose those of small thickness, and let none be slender, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap each one in two cloths according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, and let them extend from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then tie the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle; for with this structure, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, has been prepared; and let the leg be placed on it so that it is kept from movement.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound properly so called with it.
And then examine the leg every day and study it carefully, and watch it for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar problems; and if any of these problems arise, then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and what does happen to them is more attrition (wear and tear) in the case of bad disposition (meaning poor health or vulnerability).
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and those bones are elevated or displaced from each other, then the patient should place their foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on the elevated part of those bones, and then tread/press on them, and squeeze them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and lint on top, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two flat pieces are attached.
Then bind that tightly to the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary materials.
And when three or four days have passed for him, then unbind the bandage, for you will find the bones joined, or whether they are fractured or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some say they are fractured, then restore them and join them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the fractured finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little longer than it; and place under the foot that board which I have mentioned to you, and bind it with a proper bandage.
But if what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board outside the sole of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you do not forget that you consider all the things which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the woman's hip is fractured, then make her lie down in a low position, and gently incline her to one side a little, and let her be held behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made in her hip like a sphere; and then the woman should be sewn, and her hip raised little by little, because all this is expelled until the part of the hip is in front like a sphere and the fractured bone returns.
And then place powders on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and then she urinates, and reduce and return it according to the manner in which you treated it at the beginning, and then return to her first position.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and insert the whole bladder into her hip, and then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to heal.
And then it should be filled with cotton and remain so for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing that which we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these near and easily occurring fractures, especially for one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for that fracture signifies to you in the greatest dispositions the right way in its restoration and stricture, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take hen's guano and put the penis in it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and let it remain thus for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, that you attend to and phlebotomize him immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy testify to it as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to stopping it such that you powder over it crushed egg shells, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent and uncovered above the skin, then you must carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and it is an instrument which is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers, and the width of which is according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to the quantity needed for this in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the compression on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its ends; and let the top of it be of a tapering thickness, and from the middle of it to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", that is, a small spatula.
Because it is necessary that its sharp end be raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and let it be held with some device.
And then strive to align the ends of the fracture, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not receive the instrument well, then you must incise the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recounted, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors that we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient feels vehement, persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you can to give the patient great relief.
When your restoration of the bone is completed, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may incise the opening with scissors and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or festering illness, that is, gangrene, in their patients. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a dressing of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to generate pus, then you remove the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to medicate wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and closed, and it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus flows from it downwards easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not consolidated nor is the pus removed from it, then know that there are gatherings of small [things] because it is necessary to explore the wound with a probe. And of those gatherings, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated from their surroundings and cause pain in the limb, you must incise them as far as it is possible for you.
And if a swelling or ulcer occurs in the wound—that is, an abscess, or another type of corruption and putrefaction—then it is necessary that you treat each one of these with what is suitable for it, of whose cure a narration is given previously in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should address and you should keep in mind: when a large bone is broken, especially in a limb such as the thigh bone and similar large bones, and you do not attempt to pull it out, or its extraction because often death ensues from that; rather leave it until it suppurates, for it will fall out quite easily in about twenty or thirty days; and then cure the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the treatment of swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of healing of a fracture, and especially if it is near the joints, and it deforms the shape of the limb; and it quickly inhibits the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect, such as aloe, enses (likely a misspelling or archaic term, perhaps referring to a specific herb), myrrh, cerecloth, and acacia, and similar; so that you take some or all of those, and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply it to the swelling on lint, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage tight; do not loosen it for many days; and then loosen it and reapply another, until the swelling is reduced; or apply a lead plate firmly, because lead has the property of reducing anything that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted, and necessity requires its removal carefully, then incise it in the most superior part, and incise the excess and prominent part, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set, and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by several reasons: one of which is an excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightening of the ligaments so that nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or by excessive and superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the right time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and by weakness of the patient.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and make his heart cheerful so that his blood is increased; and administer baths; and introduce joy and merriment to him.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with lukewarm water until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, in a limb already restored to healing, there is crookedness and a prominence at the fractured bone and swelling, and because of this there is a deformity of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of someone who claims that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this practice is much to be condemned, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in warm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a softening plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be made and mixed with white clay or with olive oil, and a plaster should be made with it; or fat figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon droppings, and similar things from among the medicines called consolidating emollients.
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle, frequent rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb in every direction should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become severe and hardened, and surgical treatment is required, then the excess should be carefully removed and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells around the bone should be incised with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided with care and skill.
Chapter 23.
Concerning speech on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, wherefore movement is prevented, and the shape of the limb is deformed, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to immediately strive to reduce it, and not delay this reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly the reduction of the dislocation is made difficult.
And for this reason it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and then let it rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated so that its function returns to its proper place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded in fracture; from the highest part of the body to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, and this dislocation happens in one of two ways: either they are displaced from their places by slight movement, and are only slightly softened; or they are dislocated by complete and full dislocation, so that they are softened at the joint, to the point that saliva flows from the patient and they cannot retain it or close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when their dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then a reduction must be administered promptly, and it should not be delayed at all.
And let the assistant hold the patient's head, and let the physician insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or let him insert both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands should be outside with which he will pull; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then, when they are returned and pulled together, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it some cloths sprinkled with cerate, and it should be made of wax and oil mixed; and then it should be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head should be placed between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; before their meal, let their food be liquid, until when the pains are reduced and the jaws are knit together, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is knit together and healed.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and continuous thirst occur, and the patient's belly is quickly distended, and they quickly vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and many times he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards by its connection with the breastbone; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lie on his back and extend his arms. And then compress the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
And then place a plaster and powders on it and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the hip and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any way; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate to you.
And then place a plaster, and powders, and a bandage on it, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated only in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated to the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes to the lower parts below the clavicle, and especially in those whose flesh is little; it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it easily happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is believed to be dislocated because of this.
Then it is necessary that you prove this before you are completely certain of it, and then you proceed to its cure.
And its dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the clavicle; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity, and below the clavicle, towards the touch of the head of the muscle, as if it were an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands under his clavicle, and raises the joint strongly upwards to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and much time has passed since the dislocation first occurred, many days, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and that a poultice be applied that softens and relaxes, such as cooked roots of mallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should be supine on his back and a wool pad, of a consistency between soft and firm, should be placed under his clavicle; and then the physician should place his heel on the pad, and raise the head of the muscle forcefully, and pull the patient's arm so that it is not pulled downwards, and another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, for then it will immediately return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method:  that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and he should stand on the affected side, and insert his shoulder under the patient's clavicle and raise the clavicle upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's arm from under their belly. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with him to weigh him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily in another way:  a long, round-topped post, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is fixed in the ground. Then, lion-colored cloths are placed on top of the post, and the patient stands along the length of the post, and then his arm is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is pulled forcefully from the other side, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: that you take a stick, two cubits long and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be lightened at the end near the clavicle in this way:
And then, lion-colored cloths should be tied over the rounded end so that the stick does not hurt the patient.
Then it should be placed under the patient's clavicle, and the hand or arm should be extended onto the stick down to the lower parts, and the stick should be tied above the armpit and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a rung of a ladder according to its width, and the hand should be extended downwards, and the rest of the body should be suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its reduction is completed in whatever way possible for you, then you should place under the clavicle a ball of wool of suitable size; and then place a plaster prepared from ground myrtle powder and egg white over the whole muscle, so that it is wrapped above, and then tighten it below the clavicle with a decent bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and his hand should be attached to his neck, and it should be left, and his hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's diet be light until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for its healing.
And then the diet should be increased gradually over five or seven days, and movement should be exercised; and therefore if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity affecting it or another cause, then in that case cautery should be administered with three punctures, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the bandage are applied to it several times, and it is not firm and gives way and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are either severed, stretched, or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
For the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the posterior parts, reduction is done with impulsion and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another bandage be applied to it, and treat it until it is healed.
And if during healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use a bath many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former nature.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of hip dislocation.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so to the front than to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is apparent to the sight or to the touch, according to whatever manner it is dislocated. And when you adjust the dislocated hip to its place, this is manifestly clear to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the leg, nor can it touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you take care, and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the back, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and more painful, and often results in death.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's leg be extended with two hands, and the patient's thigh be extended, and the physician's hands be placed upon the hip and underneath it, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front, then return it thus: bend the leg with your hand, until you strike the hip with the base of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong and very forceful extension.
And it is necessary that two assistants extend the arm, and let those two assistants hold it firmly so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it has been wrapped with a long, doubled cloth over the patient's hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may help lubricate the joint more easily; and then push the joint with forceful pressure until it returns to its place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be applied which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be tightly bound with a descending bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And afterwards it should be unbound; and then if the joint is set in its place, then you unbind its bandage and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well secured, then you reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightly bound; and afterwards unbind it.
But if the reduction of the joint causes the patient pain, and their condition is agitated, then administer moist heat in a bath and gentle massage and compression, until they are relieved; or apply poultices of aleliati, that is, very fatty substances; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bone day and night. And afterwards remove that, and place it in a bath; and when it is sufficiently softened, then you rub the joint with a tempered massage, and afterwards reapply the aleliati, that is, very fatty ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the aid of a bath, until it is supple.
And if you wish, apply a warm, moist cow's bladder over it, tied tightly; and do this several times, for it is moist and helps to reduce the inflammation.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated wrist is sometimes easily reduced, unlike other joints. However, speed is essential in its reduction at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table, and the assistant extends the hand, and the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already facing the abdomen; then the patient places the back of their hand on a table during extension and reduction.  If the dislocation is on the back of the hand, then the hand should be placed internally on the table so that the physician's hand can reach that prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then compress it with a poultice to reduce the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not succeed in its reduction if more days pass.
However, if the joint remains distorted, and the patient experiences no pain except that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves are pinched or bruised. In this case, there is no remedy except to cauterize near the injury and quickly apply a bandage.
Therefore, when the hand is replaced, apply the poultice we have recommended, and let it remain compressed for five days; then unwrap it, let it rest, and exercise the hand.
But if its movement is impaired and some heaviness occurs, then soothe it with warm water and massage several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes fingers are dislocated in every direction.
