And when a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm or towards its belly (the front), then straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers, until it returns to place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it below the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened and aligned on that day.
And when night comes, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for some days until it is tight (healed/firm).
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts (of the body).
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death is expected for the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and many times some of their limbs are weakened, or both legs, or both arms, or one of these; or one vertebra is dislodged from its place, because many times it is dislodged, and many times the displacement is in four directions.
Therefore, that [vertebra] is dislodged to the back and is called yellowness (jaundice/possibly referring to a discoloration indicative of the severity).
But the cure is that then you consider if the yellowness (jaundice/discoloration) comes from a fever, because then in that case there is no cure nor healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from a blow or similar to those, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with long discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abridged that, the brevity of which excuses the excess of it, due to my proximity to my intended exposition, with which I have exposed and formulated this from various instruments, with which I will show this and explain that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two [sides/feet - possibly specifying a more precise location than the previous lines] specifically is cured with what I have recounted.
And it is so that the patient should be stretched out on their back against a wall, and a soft mattress should be placed underneath them so that their feet are not injured.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the edge of the bed near the patient's head, and another pole near the patient's feet at the other end of the bed.
And the assistant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another assistant should hold the other [pole] in the same manner.
And then wrap a strong linen bandage around the patient's head and neck; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is near their head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around their hips, their buttocks, and their legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is near the patient's feet.
And then each assistant should stretch the bandages to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be removed from their places where they are fixed, but however, they are not fixed as we have said [before].
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns [to its proper position], or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns [to its proper position].
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board fits. And then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a lathe which is rotated by hand; and you should place in the ground near the patient's head at the back of the neck two poles, the length of which is one arm's length, and near the patient's head at the back of the neck likewise two poles; and the distance between each pole should be one palm, and there should be a hole in each pole through which the lathe passes, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it cannot be moved.
And there should be a round pole on which the lathe is mounted, and it should be rotated in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm including that which is rotated; and the two other poles are similar to this one.
And then tighten the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet in the hole which is near their head, and the ropes that you have tied to their thighs at the lathe which is near their feet.
And then near each lathe let there be a servant who turns the lathe with his hand, and the physician manipulates the spine as we have said.
And then, after the spine is returned and the place is manipulated, then it is necessary that you apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of cloth whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is sufficient to cover the area of the curvature and the healthy part of the spine.
And then it should be bound with the rope that we have mentioned, and nourish the patient with light food until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing, then it is necessary that medicines be administered which soften and smooth, with the application of the cloth for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is then applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the spinal vertebrae, and is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made upon it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and in them does not occur what occurs in other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its dislocation inwards is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain, and this is the place where the pain is often accompanied by a hot abscess, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation outwards occurs, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of that to which dislocation forwards occurs is that he extends his leg completely, but nevertheless cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so forwards; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of that to which dislocation backwards occurs is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the hip is softened, and the head of the femur is behind that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and chronic, that it is already healed by having it close at hand you administer its reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not completely cured; and therefore it is not fitting that presumption be made in this.
But in one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it very easily returns and needs no other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand of that one from above so that he places his hands below the patient's testicles; and then tighten with a linen bandage around the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from the inside of the hip to the fork, or from the back to the back. (More literally: And let the extension be from him or from the inner part to the hip to the part of the fork, or from the back part to the part of the back.)
And let the extension of all these be at once until the patient is lifted with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type that which we shall say of its own cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is to the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligaments on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and then extend the ligament upwards from the part of the groin to the highest part of the fork (pubic area).
And then another strong assistant should take his arms, and grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with a vehement extension, because by this it will return to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, by which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is difficult for you and it does not yield to treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be tied together with a strong, light bandage, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each (bandage) be four fingers' breadth, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers' breadth.
And then the patient should be suspended by the head from a beam, and let him be inside the house; and let his length from the ground be the amount of two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong young man to lessen the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man, lessening the thigh with strength, should pull, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, the patient should lie on his back, as we have recounted in the case of yellow jaundice; and let the bandage be strongly tied above the affected leg and on his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let suitable beams be placed in the middle of the elevated part upon which wet cloths should be wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not pulled.
And then each assistant should extend to his part, and the physician with his hand should guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narrative on a raised surface; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated joint of the patient, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with that grasp he extends it downwards to the joint of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, but elevated above the ground; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two cushions; and he is on his face and the ligaments should be extended, as we have said, not very much.
And it is necessary that compression be applied with a tablet/bandage to the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is accomplished as it should be — and the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you, and that is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient straightens his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the limb is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it tightly so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and the patient should adhere to rest for three or four days.
And then untie the ligaments and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening (or weakening), then you return and plaster that, and bind that according to your first binding, and leave that for three days.
And then untie that, and make him delay in walking about for a few days until he is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards, more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration for all its types is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that he extends his leg, if he can;  apply your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then apply his knee to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee firmly, and let another assistant extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And a sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to his thigh easily and without difficulty.
And then plaster it and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if its reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with the ligatures about which the treatment of the hip has already given an account.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus.  (Talus refers to the ankle bone)
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a slight movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, extending towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight displacement is by gentle reduction, and it is that it should be extended easily with the hands and manipulated until it returns [to its place].
But in its treatment when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient lie down straight, and that a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind by the back at his middle.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from the upper side, and with your left hand from the lower side of the foot at the ankle joint.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left hand twice, and then extend with the left; and push the sole of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have described.
And then push the sole of the foot against the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then it returns in one or two attempts according to this manner, and if you see the foot set, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if reduction is forbidden to you by what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tighten a strongly fixed pulley, so that it falls between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around them so that the patient does not suffer.
And then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and then another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; and then let each attendant extend against the extension of their partner, and the pulley should be between the patient's thighs holding it back, so that their body is not pulled downwards beyond its extension.
And then the physician sets the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh lower down, because the dislocation returns easily.
And so when the dislocation is reduced and the success of the reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with bandages.
And you must be careful or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not tight and injures them severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and corrupted, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then you must administer to its healing that which we narrated before, in another place of treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
It is necessary that that which is dislocated be healed with little extension without effort; and that which is not difficult, beforehand is light/easy.
But if the dislocation is in any of the bones of the foot, then you must make the patient place his foot on a level place on the ground or on a table, and remain thus as if walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on that which protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is healed and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which are two ends, and then bind that with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then unbind it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of these things happen, and you exert yourself in its cure and in his restoration, many times death occurs.
And therefore it is not fitting that one should presume upon his cure, except he who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not-rash nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines that only subdue hot abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for that one in whom you expect salvation from loss, with alleviation of the illness, and in whom some hope appears. And then exert yourself immediately to make his reduction at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which subdues hot abscesses and cure the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and splintered bones occur, then you must exert yourself in setting them, and do in this what we have said in the simple illnesses of what precedes in those places.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into the paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for that is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary to your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
